<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Moses</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Abrahamic prophet said to have led the Israelites out of Egypt</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Moses_(disambiguation)" title="Moses (disambiguation)">Moses (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1066479718">.mw-parser-output <p><b>Moses</b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[note 1]</a></sup> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/Ë/: primary stress follows">Ë</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="/oÊ/: 'o' in 'code'">oÊ</span><span title="'z' in 'zoom'">z</span><span title="/Éª/: 'i' in 'kit'">Éª</span><span title="'z' in 'zoom'">z</span></span>,<span class="wrap"> </span>-<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="'z' in 'zoom'">z</span><span title="/Éª/: 'i' in 'kit'">Éª</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span></span>/</a></span></span>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[2]</a></sup> is considered the most important <a href="/wiki/Prophets_in_Judaism" title="Prophets in Judaism">prophet in Judaism</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aaa_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-aaa-4">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[4]</a></sup> and one of the most important <a href="/wiki/Prophets_of_Christianity" title="Prophets of Christianity">prophets in Christianity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Prophets_and_messengers_in_Islam" title="Prophets and messengers in Islam">Islam</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Druze_faith" title="Druze faith">Druze faith</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hitti_1928_37_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hitti_1928_37-6">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dana_2008_17_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dana_2008_17-7">[6]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Bah%C3%A1%CA%BC%C3%AD_Faith" title="BahÃ¡Ê¼Ã­ Faith">BahÃ¡Ê¼Ã­ Faith</a> and <a href="/wiki/Table_of_prophets_of_Abrahamic_religions" title="Table of prophets of Abrahamic religions">other Abrahamic religions</a>. According to both the <a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">Bible</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Quran</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oxism_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-oxism-8">[7]</a></sup> Moses was the leader of the <a href="/wiki/Israelites" title="Israelites">Israelites</a> and <a href="/wiki/Law_of_Moses" title="Law of Moses">lawgiver</a> to whom the <a href="/wiki/Mosaic_authorship" title="Mosaic authorship">authorship</a>, or "acquisition from heaven", of the <a href="/wiki/Torah" title="Torah">Torah</a> (the first five books of the Bible) is attributed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dever_2001_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dever_2001-9">[8]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Exodus" title="Book of Exodus">Book of Exodus</a>, Moses was born in a time when his people, the Israelites, an enslaved minority, were increasing in population and, as a result, the <a href="/wiki/Pharaohs_in_the_Bible#In_the_Book_of_Exodus" title="Pharaohs in the Bible">Egyptian Pharaoh</a> worried that they might ally themselves with Egypt's enemies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[9]</a></sup> Moses' <a href="/wiki/Hebrews" title="Hebrews">Hebrew</a> mother, <a href="/wiki/Jochebed" title="Jochebed">Jochebed</a>, secretly hid him when Pharaoh ordered all newborn Hebrew boys to be killed in order to reduce the population of the Israelites. Through Pharaoh's daughter (identified as Queen <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bithiah" title="Bithiah">Bithia</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Midrash" title="Midrash">Midrash</a>), the child was adopted as a <a href="/wiki/Child_abandonment" title="Child abandonment">foundling</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Nile" title="Nile">Nile</a> and grew up with the Egyptian royal family. After killing an Egyptian slave-master who was beating a Hebrew, Moses fled across the <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">Red Sea</a> to <a href="/wiki/Midian" title="Midian">Midian</a>, where he encountered the <a href="/wiki/Angel_of_the_Lord" title="Angel of the Lord">Angel of the Lord</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[10]</a></sup> speaking to him from within a <a href="/wiki/Burning_bush" title="Burning bush">burning bush</a> on <a href="/wiki/Mount_Horeb" title="Mount Horeb">Mount Horeb</a>, which he regarded as the Mountain of God.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/God_in_Judaism" title="God in Judaism">God</a> sent Moses back to <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> to demand the release of the Israelites from slavery. Moses said that he could not speak eloquently,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[11]</a></sup> so God allowed <a href="/wiki/Aaron" title="Aaron">Aaron</a>, his elder brother,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[12]</a></sup> to become his spokesperson. After the <a href="/wiki/Plagues_of_Egypt" title="Plagues of Egypt">Ten Plagues</a>, Moses led the <a href="/wiki/The_Exodus" title="The Exodus">Exodus of the Israelites</a> out of Egypt and <a href="/wiki/Crossing_the_Red_Sea" title="Crossing the Red Sea">across the Red Sea</a>, after which they based themselves at <a href="/wiki/Mount_Sinai_(Bible)" title="Mount Sinai (Bible)">Mount Sinai</a>, where Moses received the <a href="/wiki/Ten_Commandments" title="Ten Commandments">Ten Commandments</a>. After 40 years of wandering in the desert, Moses died on <a href="/wiki/Mount_Nebo" title="Mount Nebo">Mount Nebo</a> at the age of 120, within sight of the <a href="/wiki/Promised_Land" title="Promised Land">Promised Land</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[13]</a></sup>
</p><p>Generally, Moses is seen as a <a href="/wiki/Legend" title="Legend">legendary</a> figure, whilst retaining the possibility that Moses or a Moses-like figure existed in the 13th century BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dever2001_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dever2001-16">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Enclyc_Brit_Moses_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Enclyc_Brit_Moses-17">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miller_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Miller-19">[18]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rabbinical_Judaism" title="Rabbinical Judaism">Rabbinical Judaism</a> calculated a lifespan of Moses corresponding to 1391â1271 BCE;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[19]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Jerome" title="Jerome">Jerome</a> suggested 1592 BCE,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[20]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/James_Ussher" title="James Ussher">James Ussher</a> suggested 1571 BCE as his birth year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[note 2]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology_of_name">Etymology of name</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Moses_-_Alta-Tadema.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="635" data-file-width="1000" decoding="async" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Moses_-_Alta-Tadema.jpg/250px-Moses_-_Alta-Tadema.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Moses_-_Alta-Tadema.jpg/375px-Moses_-_Alta-Tadema.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Moses_-_Alta-Tadema.jpg/500px-Moses_-_Alta-Tadema.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Moses_-_Alta-Tadema.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/The_Finding_of_Moses_(Alma-Tadema_painting)" title="The Finding of Moses (Alma-Tadema painting)"><i>The Finding of Moses</i></a>, painting by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sir_Lawrence_Alma-Tadema" title="Sir Lawrence Alma-Tadema">Sir Lawrence Alma-Tadema</a>, 1904</div></div></div>
<p>An <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_language" title="Egyptian language">Egyptian</a> root <span title="Ancient Egyptian-language romanization"><i lang="egy-Latn"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/msj" title="wikt:msj">msy</a></i></span> ('child of') has been considered as a possible etymology, arguably an abbreviation of a <a href="/wiki/Theophoric_name" title="Theophoric name">theophoric name</a>, as for example in Egyptian names like <a href="/wiki/Thutmose" title="Thutmose">Thutmoses</a> ('child of <a href="/wiki/Thoth" title="Thoth">Thoth</a>') and <a href="/wiki/Ramesses_I" title="Ramesses I">Ramesses</a> ('child of <a href="/wiki/Ra" title="Ra">Ra</a>'),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hays_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hays-26">[24]</a></sup> with the god's name omitted. However, <a href="/wiki/Kenneth_Kitchen" title="Kenneth Kitchen">Kenneth Kitchen</a> argued that this â or any Egyptian origin for the name â was unlikely, as the sounds in the Hebrew <span title="Biblical Hebrew-language romanization"><i lang="hbo-Latn">m-Å¡-h</i></span> do not correspond to the pronunciation of Egyptian <span title="Ancient Egyptian-language romanization"><i lang="egy-Latn"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/msj" title="wikt:msj">msy</a></i></span> in the relevant time period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[25]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Yahuda" title="Abraham Yahuda">Abraham Yahuda</a>, based on the spelling given in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tanakh" title="Tanakh">Tanakh</a>, argues that it combines "water" or "seed" and "pond, expanse of water," thus yielding the sense of "child of the <a href="/wiki/Nile" title="Nile">Nile</a>" (<span title="Ancient Egyptian-language romanization"><i lang="egy-Latn"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/mw" title="wikt:mw">mw</a>-<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%C5%A1" title="wikt:Å¡">Å¡</a></i></span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>The biblical account of Moses' birth provides him with a <a href="/wiki/Folk_etymology" title="Folk etymology">folk etymology</a> to explain the ostensible meaning of his name.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hays_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hays-26">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[27]</a></sup> He is said to have received it from the Pharaoh's daughter: "he became her son. She named him Moses [<span title="Biblical Hebrew-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="hbo">×Ö¹×©Ö¶××</span></span>, <span title="Biblical Hebrew-language romanization"><i lang="hbo-Latn">MÅÅ¡e</i></span>], saying, 'I drew him out [<span title="Biblical Hebrew-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="hbo">×Ö°×©Ö´×××ªÖ´Ö½××Ö¼</span></span>, <span title="Biblical Hebrew-language romanization"><i lang="hbo-Latn">mÇÅ¡Ä«á¹¯Ä«hÅ«</i></span>] of the water'."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MaciÃ¡_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-MaciÃ¡-31">[29]</a></sup> This explanation links it to the <a href="/wiki/Semitic_languages" title="Semitic languages">Semitic</a> root <span title="Semitic languages collective text"><span dir="rtl" lang="sem">××©××</span></span>, <span title="Semitic languages romanization"><i lang="sem-Latn">m-Å¡-h</i></span>, meaning "to draw out".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MaciÃ¡_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-MaciÃ¡-31">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDozeman200981â82_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDozeman200981â82-32">[30]</a></sup> The eleventh-century <a href="/wiki/Tosafot" title="Tosafot">Tosafist</a> <a href="/wiki/Isaac_ben_Asher_ha-Levi" title="Isaac ben Asher ha-Levi">Isaac b. Asher haLevi</a> noted that the princess names him the active participle 'drawer-out' (<span title="Biblical Hebrew-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="hbo">×Ö¹×©Ö¶××</span></span>, <span title="Biblical Hebrew-language romanization"><i lang="hbo-Latn">mÅÅ¡e</i></span>), not the passive participle 'drawn-out' (<span title="Biblical Hebrew-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="hbo">× Ö´×Ö°×©Ö¶××</span></span>, <span title="Biblical Hebrew-language romanization"><i lang="hbo-Latn">nÄ«mÅ¡e</i></span>), in effect prophesying that Moses would draw others out (of Egypt); this has been accepted by some scholars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greifenhagen_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Greifenhagen-34">[32]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_language" title="Hebrew language">Hebrew</a> etymology in the Biblical story may reflect an attempt to cancel out traces of Moses' <a href="/wiki/Egyptians" title="Egyptians">Egyptian origins</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greifenhagen_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-Greifenhagen-34">[32]</a></sup> The Egyptian character of his name was recognized as such by ancient Jewish writers like <a href="/wiki/Philo" title="Philo">Philo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Josephus" title="Josephus">Josephus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greifenhagen_34-2"><a href="#cite_note-Greifenhagen-34">[32]</a></sup> Philo linked Moses's name (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_language" title="Ancient Greek language">Ancient Greek</a>: <span lang="grc">ÎÏÏÏá¿Ï</span>, <small><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Romanization_of_Ancient_Greek" title="Romanization of Ancient Greek">romanized</a>:Â </small><span title="Ancient Greek-language romanization"><i lang="grc-Latn">MÅysÄs</i></span>, <small><a href="/wiki/Literal_translation" title="Literal translation">lit.</a>â</small>'MÅusá¸s') to the Egyptian (<a href="/wiki/Coptic_language" title="Coptic language">Coptic</a>) word for 'water' (<span title="Coptic-language romanization"><i lang="cop-Latn">mÃ¶u</i></span>, <span title="Coptic-language text"><span lang="cop">Î¼á¿¶Ï</span></span>), in reference to his finding in the Nile and the biblical <a href="/wiki/Folk_etymology" title="Folk etymology">folk etymology</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[note 3]</a></sup> Josephus, in his <i><a href="/wiki/Antiquities_of_the_Jews" title="Antiquities of the Jews">Antiquities of the Jews</a></i>, claims that the second element, <span title="Biblical Hebrew-language romanization"><i lang="hbo-Latn">-esÃªs</i></span>, meant 'those who are saved'. The problem of how an Egyptian princess, known to Josephus as Thermutis (identified as Tharmuth)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MaciÃ¡_31-2"><a href="#cite_note-MaciÃ¡-31">[29]</a></sup> and to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1_Chronicles" title="1 Chronicles">1 Chronicles</a> 4:18 as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bithiah" title="Bithiah">Bithiah</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[33]</a></sup> could have known Hebrew puzzled medieval Jewish commentators like <a href="/wiki/Abraham_ibn_Ezra" title="Abraham ibn Ezra">Abraham ibn Ezra</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hezekiah_ben_Manoah" title="Hezekiah ben Manoah">Hezekiah ben Manoah</a>. Hezekiah suggested she either converted or took a tip from <a href="/wiki/Jochebed" title="Jochebed">Jochebed</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Salkin_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Salkin-37">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[35]</a></sup>
</p><p>Ibn Ezra gave two possibilities for the name of Moses, he believed that it was either a translation of the Egyptian name instead of a transliteration, or that the Pharaoh's daughter was able to speak Hebrew.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[37]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Biblical_narrative">Biblical narrative</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SyriacBibleParisFolio8rrMosesBeforePharaoh.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1789" data-file-width="1470" decoding="async" height="268" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/SyriacBibleParisFolio8rrMosesBeforePharaoh.jpg/220px-SyriacBibleParisFolio8rrMosesBeforePharaoh.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/SyriacBibleParisFolio8rrMosesBeforePharaoh.jpg/330px-SyriacBibleParisFolio8rrMosesBeforePharaoh.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/SyriacBibleParisFolio8rrMosesBeforePharaoh.jpg/440px-SyriacBibleParisFolio8rrMosesBeforePharaoh.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SyriacBibleParisFolio8rrMosesBeforePharaoh.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Moses before the <a href="/wiki/Pharaohs_in_the_Bible#In_the_Book_of_Exodus" title="Pharaohs in the Bible">Pharaoh</a>, a 6th-century miniature from the <a href="/wiki/Syriac_Bible_of_Paris" title="Syriac Bible of Paris">Syriac Bible of Paris</a></div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prophet_and_deliverer_of_Israel">Prophet and deliverer of Israel</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/The_Exodus" title="The Exodus">The Exodus</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Israelites" title="Israelites">Israelites</a> had settled in the <a href="/wiki/Land_of_Goshen" title="Land of Goshen">Land of Goshen</a> in the time of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Joseph_(patriarch)" title="Joseph (patriarch)">Joseph</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jacob" title="Jacob">Jacob</a>, but a new <a href="/wiki/Pharaohs_in_the_Bible#In_the_Book_of_Exodus" title="Pharaohs in the Bible">Pharaoh</a> arose who oppressed the children of <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a>. At this time Moses was born to his father <a href="/wiki/Amram" title="Amram">Amram</a>, son (or descendant) of <a href="/wiki/Kehath" title="Kehath">Kehath</a> the <a href="/wiki/Levite" title="Levite">Levite</a>, who entered Egypt with Jacob's household; his mother was <a href="/wiki/Jochebed" title="Jochebed">Jochebed</a> (also Yocheved), who was kin to Kehath. Moses had one older (by seven years) sister, <a href="/wiki/Miriam" title="Miriam">Miriam</a>, and one older (by three years) brother, <a href="/wiki/Aaron" title="Aaron">Aaron</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[39]</a></sup> Pharaoh had commanded that all male Hebrew children born would be drowned in the river <a href="/wiki/Nile" title="Nile">Nile</a>, but Moses' mother placed him in an <a href="/wiki/Ark_of_bulrushes" title="Ark of bulrushes">ark</a> and concealed the ark in the <a href="/wiki/Bulrush" title="Bulrush">bulrushes</a> by the riverbank, where <a href="/wiki/Finding_of_Moses" title="Finding of Moses">the baby was discovered</a> and adopted by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bithiah" title="Bithiah">Pharaoh's daughter</a>, and raised as an Egyptian. One day, after Moses had reached adulthood, he killed an Egyptian who was beating a Hebrew. Moses, in order to escape Pharaoh's <a href="/wiki/Capital_punishment" title="Capital punishment">death penalty</a>, fled to <a href="/wiki/Midian" title="Midian">Midian</a> (a desert country south of Judah), where he married <a href="/wiki/Zipporah" title="Zipporah">Zipporah</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[40]</a></sup>
</p><p>There, on <a href="/wiki/Mount_Horeb" title="Mount Horeb">Mount Horeb</a>, <a href="/wiki/God" title="God">God</a> appeared to Moses as a <a href="/wiki/Burning_bush" title="Burning bush">burning bush</a>, revealed to Moses his name <a href="/wiki/Tetragrammaton" title="Tetragrammaton">YHWH</a> (probably pronounced <a href="/wiki/Yahweh" title="Yahweh">Yahweh</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[41]</a></sup> and commanded him to return to Egypt and bring his <a href="/wiki/Jews_as_the_chosen_people" title="Jews as the chosen people">chosen people</a> (Israel) out of bondage and into the <a href="/wiki/Promised_Land" title="Promised Land">Promised Land</a> (<a href="/wiki/Canaan" title="Canaan">Canaan</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[43]</a></sup> During the journey, God tried to kill Moses,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[44]</a></sup> but <a href="/wiki/Zipporah_at_the_inn" title="Zipporah at the inn">Zipporah saved his life</a>. Moses returned to carry out God's command, but God caused the Pharaoh to refuse, and only after God had subjected Egypt to <a href="/wiki/Plagues_of_Egypt" title="Plagues of Egypt">ten plagues</a> did Pharaoh relent. Moses led the Israelites to the border of Egypt, but there God hardened the Pharaoh's heart once more, so that he could destroy Pharaoh and his army at the <a href="/wiki/Crossing_the_Red_Sea" title="Crossing the Red Sea">Red Sea Crossing</a> as a sign of his power to Israel and the nations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[45]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:VictoryOLord.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2490" data-file-width="1780" decoding="async" height="308" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/VictoryOLord.JPG/220px-VictoryOLord.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/VictoryOLord.JPG/330px-VictoryOLord.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/VictoryOLord.JPG/440px-VictoryOLord.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:VictoryOLord.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Victory_O_Lord!" title="Victory O Lord!">Victory O Lord!</a></i>, 1871 painting by <a href="/wiki/John_Everett_Millais" title="John Everett Millais">John Everett Millais</a>, depicts Moses holding his <a href="/wiki/Staff_of_Moses" title="Staff of Moses">staff</a>, assisted by <a href="/wiki/Aaron" title="Aaron">Aaron</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hur_(Bible)" title="Hur (Bible)">Hur</a>, holding up his arms during the battle against <a href="/wiki/Amalek" title="Amalek">Amalek</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>After defeating the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amalekites" title="Amalekites">Amalekites</a> in <a href="/wiki/Rephidim" title="Rephidim">Rephidim</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[46]</a></sup> Moses <a href="/wiki/The_Exodus" title="The Exodus">led the Israelites</a> to <a href="/wiki/Mount_Sinai_(Bible)" title="Mount Sinai (Bible)">Mount Sinai</a>, where he was given the <a href="/wiki/Ten_Commandments" title="Ten Commandments">Ten Commandments</a> from God, written on <a href="/wiki/Stele" title="Stele">stone tablets</a>. However, since Moses remained a long time on the mountain, some of the people feared that he might be dead, so they made a statue of a <a href="/wiki/Golden_calf" title="Golden calf">golden calf</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sacred_bull" title="Sacred bull">worshipped it</a>, thus disobeying and angering God and Moses. Moses, out of anger, broke the tablets, and later ordered the elimination of those who had worshiped the golden statue, which was melted down and fed to the <a href="/wiki/Idolatry" title="Idolatry">idolaters</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[47]</a></sup> He also wrote the ten commandments on a new set of tablets. Later at <a href="/wiki/Mount_Sinai" title="Mount Sinai">Mount Sinai</a>, Moses and the elders entered into a covenant, by which Israel would become the people of YHWH, obeying his laws, and YHWH would be their god. Moses delivered the laws of God to Israel, instituted <a href="/wiki/Kohen" title="Kohen">the priesthood</a> under the sons of Moses' brother <a href="/wiki/Aaron" title="Aaron">Aaron</a>, and destroyed those Israelites who fell away from his worship. In his final act at Sinai, God gave Moses instructions for the <a href="/wiki/Tabernacle" title="Tabernacle">Tabernacle</a>, the mobile shrine by which he would travel with Israel to the Promised Land.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[48]</a></sup>
</p><p>From Sinai, Moses led the Israelites to the <a href="/wiki/Desert_of_Paran" title="Desert of Paran">Desert of Paran</a> on the border of Canaan. From there he sent <a href="/wiki/The_Twelve_Spies" title="The Twelve Spies">twelve spies</a> into the land. The spies returned with samples of the land's fertility, but warned that its inhabitants were <a href="/wiki/Nephilim" title="Nephilim">giants</a>. The people were afraid and wanted to return to Egypt, and some rebelled against Moses and against God. Moses told the Israelites that they were not worthy to inherit the land, and would wander the wilderness for forty years until the generation who had refused to enter Canaan had died, so that it would be their children who would possess the land.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[49]</a></sup> Later on, <a href="/wiki/Korah" title="Korah">Korah</a> was punished for leading a revolt against Moses.
</p><p>When the forty years had passed, Moses led the Israelites east around the <a href="/wiki/Dead_Sea" title="Dead Sea">Dead Sea</a> to the territories of <a href="/wiki/Edom" title="Edom">Edom</a> and <a href="/wiki/Moab" title="Moab">Moab</a>. There they escaped the temptation of idolatry, conquered the lands of <a href="/wiki/Og" title="Og">Og</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sihon" title="Sihon">Sihon</a> in <a href="/wiki/Transjordan_(region)" title="Transjordan (region)">Transjordan</a>, received God's blessing through <a href="/wiki/Balaam" title="Balaam">Balaam</a> the prophet, and massacred the <a href="/wiki/Midian" title="Midian">Midianites</a>, who by the end of the Exodus journey had become the enemies of the Israelites due to their notorious role in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heresy_of_Peor" title="Heresy of Peor">enticing the Israelites to sin against God</a>. Moses was twice given notice that he would die before entry to the Promised Land: in <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Numbers" title="Book of Numbers">Numbers</a> 27:13,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[50]</a></sup> once he had seen the Promised Land from a viewpoint on <a href="/wiki/Abarim" title="Abarim">Mount Abarim</a>, and again in Numbers 31:1<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[51]</a></sup> once battle with the Midianites had been won.
</p><p>On the banks of the <a href="/wiki/Jordan_River" title="Jordan River">Jordan River</a>, in sight of the land, Moses assembled the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tribes_of_Israel#The_Twelve_Tribes" title="Tribes of Israel">tribes</a>. After recalling their wanderings he delivered God's laws by which they must live in the land, sang a <a href="/wiki/Song_of_Moses" title="Song of Moses">song</a> of praise and pronounced a <a href="/wiki/Blessing_of_Moses" title="Blessing of Moses">blessing</a> on the people, and passed his authority to <a href="/wiki/Joshua" title="Joshua">Joshua</a>, under whom they would possess the land. Moses then went up <a href="/wiki/Mount_Nebo" title="Mount Nebo">Mount Nebo</a>, looked over the <a href="/wiki/Promised_Land" title="Promised Land">Promised Land</a> spread out before him, and died, at the age of one hundred and twenty.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lawgiver_of_Israel">Lawgiver of Israel</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Law_of_Moses" title="Law of Moses">Law of Moses</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mosaic_authorship" title="Mosaic authorship">Mosaic authorship</a>, <a href="/wiki/Deuteronomist" title="Deuteronomist">Deuteronomist</a>, <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Deuteronomy#Deuteronomic_code" title="Book of Deuteronomy">Book of Deuteronomy Â§Â Deuteronomic code</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/613_Mitzvot" title="613 Mitzvot">613 Mitzvot</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rembrandt_-_Moses_Smashing_the_Tablets_of_the_Law_-_WGA19132.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1230" data-file-width="1000" decoding="async" height="271" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Rembrandt_-_Moses_Smashing_the_Tablets_of_the_Law_-_WGA19132.jpg/220px-Rembrandt_-_Moses_Smashing_the_Tablets_of_the_Law_-_WGA19132.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Rembrandt_-_Moses_Smashing_the_Tablets_of_the_Law_-_WGA19132.jpg/330px-Rembrandt_-_Moses_Smashing_the_Tablets_of_the_Law_-_WGA19132.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Rembrandt_-_Moses_Smashing_the_Tablets_of_the_Law_-_WGA19132.jpg/440px-Rembrandt_-_Moses_Smashing_the_Tablets_of_the_Law_-_WGA19132.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rembrandt_-_Moses_Smashing_the_Tablets_of_the_Law_-_WGA19132.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Moses_Breaking_the_Tablets_of_the_Law" title="Moses Breaking the Tablets of the Law">Moses Breaking the Tablets of the Law</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Rembrandt" title="Rembrandt">Rembrandt</a>, 1659</div></div></div>
<p>Moses is honoured among <a href="/wiki/Jews" title="Jews">Jews</a> today as the "lawgiver of Israel", and he delivers several sets of laws in the course of the four books. The first is the <a href="/wiki/Covenant_Code" title="Covenant Code">Covenant Code</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[52]</a></sup> the terms of the <a href="/wiki/Mosaic_covenant" title="Mosaic covenant">covenant</a> which God offers to the Israelites at Mount Sinai. Embedded in the covenant are the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Decalogue" title="Decalogue">Decalogue</a> (the <a href="/wiki/Ten_Commandments" title="Ten Commandments">Ten Commandments</a>, Exodus 20:1â17),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[53]</a></sup> and the Book of the Covenant (Exodus 20:22â23:19).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHamilton2011xxv_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHamilton2011xxv-58">[55]</a></sup> The entire <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Leviticus" title="Book of Leviticus">Book of Leviticus</a> constitutes a second body of law, the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Numbers" title="Book of Numbers">Book of Numbers</a> begins with yet another set, and the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Deuteronomy" title="Book of Deuteronomy">Book of Deuteronomy</a> another.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="What is the primary source for this? (June 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Moses has traditionally been regarded as <a href="/wiki/Mosaic_authorship" title="Mosaic authorship">the author of those four books</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Genesis" title="Book of Genesis">Book of Genesis</a>, which together comprise the <a href="/wiki/Torah" title="Torah">Torah</a>, the first section of the <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_Bible" title="Hebrew Bible">Hebrew Bible</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[56]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Historicity">Historicity</span></h2>
<p>Scholars hold different opinions on the status of Moses in scholarship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nigo1993_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nigo1993-60">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Archaeo_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Archaeo-61">[58]</a></sup> For instance, according to <a href="/wiki/William_G._Dever" title="William G. Dever">William G. Dever</a>, the modern scholarly consensus is that the biblical person of Moses is largely mythical while also holding that "a Moses-like figure may have existed somewhere in the southern <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transjordan_(Bible)" title="Transjordan (Bible)">Transjordan</a> in the mid-late 13th century B.C." and that "archeology can do nothing" to prove or confirm either way.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Archaeo_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-Archaeo-61">[58]</a></sup> However, according to Solomon Nigosian, there are actually three prevailing views among biblical scholars: one is that Moses is not a historical figure, another view strives to anchor the decisive role he played in Israelite religion, and a third that argues there are elements of both history and legend from which "these issues are hotly debated unresolved matters among scholars".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nigo1993_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nigo1993-60">[57]</a></sup> According to Brian Britt, there is divide amongst scholars when discussing matters on Moses that threatens gridlock.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britt_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Britt-63">[60]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Jan_Assmann" title="Jan Assmann">Jan Assmann</a> argues that it cannot be known if Moses ever lived because there are no traces of him outside tradition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[61]</a></sup> Though the names of Moses and others in the biblical narratives are Egyptian and contain genuine Egyptian elements, no extrabiblical sources point clearly to Moses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dever_2008_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dever_2008-65">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oxford_Bib_Studies_Moses_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Oxford_Bib_Studies_Moses-67">[64]</a></sup> No references to Moses appear in any Egyptian sources prior to the fourth century BCE, long after he is believed to have lived. No contemporary Egyptian sources mention Moses, or the events of ExodusâDeuteronomy, nor has any archaeological evidence been discovered in Egypt or the <a href="/wiki/Sinai_Peninsula" title="Sinai Peninsula">Sinai wilderness</a> to support the story in which he is the central figure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeyers20055â6_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeyers20055â6-68">[65]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/David_Adams_Leeming" title="David Adams Leeming">David Adams Leeming</a> states that Moses is a mythic hero and the central figure in Hebrew mythology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[66]</a></sup>
The <i>Oxford Companion to the Bible</i> states that the historicity of Moses is the most reasonable (albeit not unbiased) assumption be made about him as his absence would leave a vacuum that cannot be explained away.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[67]</a></sup> <i>Oxford Biblical Studies</i> states that although few modern scholars are willing to support the traditional view that Moses himself wrote the five books of the <a href="/wiki/Torah" title="Torah">Torah</a>, there are certainly those who regard the leadership of Moses as too firmly based in Israel's corporate memory to be dismissed as pious fiction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[68]</a></sup>
</p><p>The story of Moses's discovery picks up a familiar motif in <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Near_East" title="Ancient Near East">ancient Near Eastern</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mythology" title="Mythology">mythological accounts</a> of the ruler who rises from humble origins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[69]</a></sup> Thus <a href="/wiki/Sargon_of_Akkad" title="Sargon of Akkad">Sargon of Akkad</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Akkadian_language" title="Akkadian language">Akkadian</a> account of his own origins runs:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><div class="poem">
<p>My mother, the high priestess, conceived; in secret she bore me<br/>
She set me in a basket of rushes, with bitumen she sealed my lid<br/>
She cast me into the river which rose over me.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[70]</a></sup>
</p>
</div></blockquote>
<p>Moses's story, like those of the other patriarchs, most likely had a substantial oral prehistory<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPitard200127_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPitard200127-74">[71]</a></sup> (he is mentioned in the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Jeremiah" title="Book of Jeremiah">Book of Jeremiah</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[72]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Isaiah" title="Book of Isaiah">Book of Isaiah</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[73]</a></sup> and his name is apparently very ancient, as the tradition found in Exodus no longer understands its original meaning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hays_26-2"><a href="#cite_note-Hays-26">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThompson200223â24_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThompson200223â24-77">[74]</a></sup> Nevertheless, the completion of the Torah and its elevation to the centre of post-Exilic Judaism was as much or more about combining older texts as writing new ones â the final Pentateuch was based on existing traditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarrConway2010193_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarrConway2010193-78">[75]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Isaiah" title="Book of Isaiah">Isaiah</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[76]</a></sup> written during the Exile (i.e., in the first half of the 6th century BCE), testifies to tension between the people of Judah and the returning post-Exilic Jews (the "<a href="/wiki/Golah" title="Golah">gÃ´lÃ¢</a>"), stating that God is the father of Israel and that Israel's history begins with the Exodus and not with <a href="/wiki/Abraham" title="Abraham">Abraham</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESka200944_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESka200944-80">[77]</a></sup> The conclusion to be inferred from this and similar evidence (e.g., the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Ezra" title="Book of Ezra">Book of Ezra</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Nehemiah" title="Book of Nehemiah">Book of Nehemiah</a>) is that the figure of Moses and the story of the Exodus must have been preeminent among the people of Judah at the time of the Exile and after, serving to support their claims to the land in opposition to those of the returning exiles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESka200944_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESka200944-80">[77]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Kenite_hypothesis" title="Kenite hypothesis">A theory</a> developed by <a href="/wiki/Cornelis_Tiele" title="Cornelis Tiele">Cornelis Tiele</a> in 1872, which has proved influential, argued that <a href="/wiki/Yahweh" title="Yahweh">Yahweh</a> was a <a href="/wiki/Midian" title="Midian">Midianite</a> god, introduced to the Israelites by Moses, whose father-in-law <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jethro_(Bible)" title="Jethro (Bible)">Jethro</a> was a Midianite priest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[78]</a></sup> It was to such a Moses that Yahweh reveals his real name, hidden from the <a href="/wiki/Patriarchs_(Bible)" title="Patriarchs (Bible)">Patriarchs</a> who knew him only as <a href="/wiki/El_Shaddai" title="El Shaddai">El Shaddai</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[79]</a></sup> Against this view is the modern consensus that most of the Israelites were native to <a href="/wiki/Palestine_(region)" title="Palestine (region)">Palestine</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[80]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Martin_Noth" title="Martin Noth">Martin Noth</a> argued that the <a href="/wiki/Torah" title="Torah">Pentateuch</a> uses the figure of Moses, originally linked to legends of a Transjordan conquest, as a narrative bracket or late redactional device to weld together four of the five, originally independent, themes of that work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Coats_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-Coats-84">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[82]</a></sup> <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Manfred_G%C3%B6rg&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Manfred GÃ¶rg (page does not exist)">Manfred GÃ¶rg</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;">Â [<a class="extiw" href="https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Manfred_G%C3%B6rg" title="de:Manfred GÃ¶rg">de</a>]</span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[83]</a></sup> and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Rolf_Krauss_(egyptologist)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Rolf Krauss (egyptologist) (page does not exist)">Rolf Krauss</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;">Â [<a class="extiw" href="https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rolf_Krauss" title="de:Rolf Krauss">de</a>]</span>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[84]</a></sup> the latter in a somewhat <a href="/wiki/Sensationalism" title="Sensationalism">sensationalist</a> manner,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[85]</a></sup> have suggested that the Moses story is a distortion or transmogrification of the historical pharaoh <a href="/wiki/Amenmesse" title="Amenmesse">Amenmose</a> (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;">â1200 BCE</span>), who was dismissed from office and whose name was later simplified to <span title="Ancient Egyptian-language romanization"><i lang="egy-Latn">msy</i></span> (Mose). <a href="/wiki/Aidan_Dodson" title="Aidan Dodson">Aidan Dodson</a> regards this hypothesis as "intriguing, but beyond proof".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[86]</a></sup> Rudolf Smend argues that the two details about Moses that were most likely to be historical are his name, of Egyptian origin, and his marriage to a Midianite woman, details which seem unlikely to have been invented by the Israelites; in Smend's view, all other details given in the biblical narrative are too mythically charged to be seen as accurate data.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[87]</a></sup>
</p><p>The name <a href="/wiki/Mesha" title="Mesha">King Mesha</a> of <a href="/wiki/Moab" title="Moab">Moab</a> has been linked to that of Moses. Mesha also is associated with narratives of an exodus and a conquest, and several motifs in stories about him are shared with the Exodus tale and that regarding Israel's war with Moab (<a href="/wiki/2_Kings_3" title="2 Kings 3">2 Kings 3</a>). Moab rebels against oppression, like Moses, leads his people out of Israel, as Moses does from Egypt, and his first-born son is slaughtered at the wall of <a href="/wiki/Kir_of_Moab" title="Kir of Moab">Kir-hareseth</a> as the firstborn of Israel are condemned to slaughter in the Exodus story, in what Calvinist theologian <a href="/wiki/Peter_Leithart" title="Peter Leithart">Peter Leithart</a> described as "an infernal Passover that delivers Mesha while wrath burns against his enemies".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[88]</a></sup>
</p><p>An Egyptian version of the tale that crosses over with the Moses story is found in <a href="/wiki/Manetho" title="Manetho">Manetho</a> who, according to the summary in <a href="/wiki/Josephus" title="Josephus">Josephus</a>, wrote that a certain <a href="/wiki/Osarseph" title="Osarseph">Osarseph</a>, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heliopolis_(Ancient_Egypt)" title="Heliopolis (Ancient Egypt)">Heliopolitan</a> priest, became overseer of a band of <a href="/wiki/Leprosy" title="Leprosy">lepers</a>, when <a href="/wiki/Amenhotep_III" title="Amenhotep III">Amenophis</a>, following indications by <a href="/wiki/Amenhotep,_son_of_Hapu" title="Amenhotep, son of Hapu">Amenhotep, son of Hapu</a>, had all the lepers in Egypt quarantined in order to cleanse the land so that he might see the gods. The lepers are bundled into <a href="/wiki/Avaris" title="Avaris">Avaris</a>, the former capital of the <a href="/wiki/Hyksos" title="Hyksos">Hyksos</a>, where Osarseph prescribes for them everything forbidden in Egypt, while proscribing everything permitted in Egypt. They invite the Hyksos to reinvade Egypt, rule with them for 13 years â Osarseph then assumes the name Moses â and are then driven out.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[89]</a></sup>
</p><p>Other Egyptian figures which have been postulated as candidates for a historical Moses-like figure include the princes <a href="/wiki/Ahmose-ankh" title="Ahmose-ankh">Ahmose-ankh</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ramose_(prince)" title="Ramose (prince)">Ramose</a>, who were sons of pharaoh <a href="/wiki/Ahmose_I" title="Ahmose I">Ahmose I</a>, or a figure associated with the family of pharaoh <a href="/wiki/Thutmose_III" title="Thutmose III">Thutmose III</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[91]</a></sup> Israel Knohl has proposed to identify Moses with <a href="/wiki/Irsu" title="Irsu">Irsu</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Shasu" title="Shasu">Shasu</a> who, according to <a href="/wiki/Papyrus_Harris_I" title="Papyrus Harris I">Papyrus Harris I</a> and the Elephantine Stele, took power in Egypt with the support of "Asiatics" (people from the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a>) after the death of Queen <a href="/wiki/Twosret" title="Twosret">Twosret</a>; after coming to power, Irsu and his supporters disrupted Egyptian rituals, "treating the gods like the people" and halting offerings to the Egyptian deities. They were eventually defeated and expelled by the new Pharaoh <a href="/wiki/Setnakhte" title="Setnakhte">Setnakhte</a> and, while fleeing, they abandoned large quantities of gold and silver they had stolen from the temples.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[92]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Hellenistic_literature">Hellenistic literature</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Moses_in_Judeo-Hellenistic_literature" title="Moses in Judeo-Hellenistic literature">Moses in Judeo-Hellenistic literature</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Memorial_of_Moses,_Mt._Nebo.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="561" data-file-width="574" decoding="async" height="215" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Memorial_of_Moses%2C_Mt._Nebo.jpg/220px-Memorial_of_Moses%2C_Mt._Nebo.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Memorial_of_Moses%2C_Mt._Nebo.jpg/330px-Memorial_of_Moses%2C_Mt._Nebo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Memorial_of_Moses%2C_Mt._Nebo.jpg/440px-Memorial_of_Moses%2C_Mt._Nebo.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Memorial_of_Moses,_Mt._Nebo.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Memorial of Moses, <a href="/wiki/Mount_Nebo" title="Mount Nebo">Mount Nebo</a>, Jordan</div></div></div>
<p>Non-biblical writings about Jews, with references to the role of Moses, first appear at the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_period" title="Hellenistic period">Hellenistic period</a>, from 323Â BCE to about 146Â BCE. Shmuel notes that "a characteristic of this literature is the high honour in which it holds the peoples of the East in general and some specific groups among these peoples."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShmuel19761102_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShmuel19761102-96">[93]</a></sup>
</p><p>In addition to the Judeo-Roman or Judeo-Hellenic historians <a href="/wiki/Artapanus_of_Alexandria" title="Artapanus of Alexandria">Artapanus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eupolemus" title="Eupolemus">Eupolemus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Josephus" title="Josephus">Josephus</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Philo" title="Philo">Philo</a>, a few non-Jewish historians including <a href="/wiki/Hecataeus_of_Abdera" title="Hecataeus of Abdera">Hecataeus of Abdera</a> (quoted by <a href="/wiki/Diodorus_Siculus" title="Diodorus Siculus">Diodorus Siculus</a>), <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Polyhistor" title="Alexander Polyhistor">Alexander Polyhistor</a>, <a href="/wiki/Manetho" title="Manetho">Manetho</a>, <a href="/wiki/Apion" title="Apion">Apion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chaeremon_of_Alexandria" title="Chaeremon of Alexandria">Chaeremon of Alexandria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tacitus" title="Tacitus">Tacitus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Porphyry_(philosopher)" title="Porphyry (philosopher)">Porphyry</a> also make reference to him. The extent to which any of these accounts rely on earlier sources is unknown.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShmuel19761103_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShmuel19761103-97">[94]</a></sup> Moses also appears in other religious texts such as the <a href="/wiki/Mishnah" title="Mishnah">Mishnah</a> (c.Â 200Â CE) and the <a href="/wiki/Midrash" title="Midrash">Midrash</a> (200â1200Â CE).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[95]</a></sup>
</p><p>The figure of <a href="/wiki/Osarseph" title="Osarseph">Osarseph</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_historiography" title="Greek historiography">Hellenistic historiography</a> is a renegade Egyptian priest who leads an army of lepers against the pharaoh and is finally expelled from Egypt, changing his name to Moses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[96]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hecataeus">Hecataeus</span></h3>
<p>The earliest existing reference to Moses in Greek literature occurs in the Egyptian history of Hecataeus of Abdera (4th century BCE). All that remains of his description of Moses are two references made by Diodorus Siculus, wherein, writes historian Arthur Droge, he "describes Moses as a wise and courageous leader who left Egypt and colonized <a href="/wiki/Judea" title="Judea">Judaea</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDroge198918_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDroge198918-100">[97]</a></sup> Among the many accomplishments described by Hecataeus, Moses had founded cities, established a temple and religious cult, and issued laws:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>After the establishment of settled life in Egypt in early times, which took place, according to the mythical account, in the period of the gods and heroes, the firstÂ ... to persuade the multitudes to use written laws was Mneves, a man not only great of soul but also in his life the most public-spirited of all lawgivers whose names are recorded.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDroge198918_100-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDroge198918-100">[97]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Droge also points out that this statement by Hecataeus was similar to statements made subsequently by Eupolemus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDroge198918_100-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDroge198918-100">[97]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Artapanus">Artapanus</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Knesset_Menorah_P5200010_Moses.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="660" data-file-width="420" decoding="async" height="346" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/The_Knesset_Menorah_P5200010_Moses.JPG/220px-The_Knesset_Menorah_P5200010_Moses.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/The_Knesset_Menorah_P5200010_Moses.JPG/330px-The_Knesset_Menorah_P5200010_Moses.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/8a/The_Knesset_Menorah_P5200010_Moses.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Knesset_Menorah_P5200010_Moses.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Depiction of Moses on the <a href="/wiki/Knesset_Menorah" title="Knesset Menorah">Knesset Menorah</a> raising his arms during the battle against the Amalekites</div></div></div>
<p>The Jewish historian <a href="/wiki/Artapanus_of_Alexandria" title="Artapanus of Alexandria">Artapanus of Alexandria</a> (2nd century BCE), portrayed Moses as a cultural hero, alien to the Pharaonic court. According to theologian John Barclay, the Moses of Artapanus "clearly bears the destiny of the Jews, and in his personal, cultural and military splendor, brings credit to the whole Jewish people".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[98]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p> Jealousy of Moses' excellent qualities induced Chenephres to send him with unskilled troops on a military expedition to <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a>, where he won great victories. After having built the city of <a href="/wiki/Hermopolis" title="Hermopolis">Hermopolis</a>, he taught the people the value of the <a href="/wiki/Ibis" title="Ibis">ibis</a> as a protection against the serpents, making the bird the sacred guardian spirit of the city; then he introduced <a href="/wiki/Circumcision" title="Circumcision">circumcision</a>. After his return to <a href="/wiki/Memphis,_Egypt" title="Memphis, Egypt">Memphis</a>, Moses taught the people the value of oxen for agriculture, and the consecration of the same by Moses gave rise to the cult of <a href="/wiki/Apis_(deity)" title="Apis (deity)">Apis</a>. Finally, after having escaped another plot by killing the assailant sent by the king, Moses fled to <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Peninsula" title="Arabian Peninsula">Arabia</a>, where he married the daughter of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jethro_(Bible)" title="Jethro (Bible)">Raguel</a> [Jethro], the ruler of the district.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[99]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Artapanus goes on to relate how Moses returns to Egypt with Aaron, and is imprisoned, but miraculously escapes through the name of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/YHWH" title="YHWH">YHWH</a> in order to lead the Exodus. This account further testifies that all Egyptian <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_temple" title="Egyptian temple">temples</a> of <a href="/wiki/Isis" title="Isis">Isis</a> thereafter contained a rod, in remembrance of that used for Moses's miracles. He describes Moses as 80 years old, "tall and ruddy, with long white hair, and dignified".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[100]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some historians, however, point out the "<a href="/wiki/Apologetics" title="Apologetics">apologetic</a> nature of much of Artapanus' work",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFeldman199840_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFeldman199840-104">[101]</a></sup> with his addition of extra-biblical details, such as his references to Jethro: the non-Jewish Jethro expresses admiration for Moses's gallantry in helping his daughters, and chooses to adopt Moses as his son.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFeldman1998133_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFeldman1998133-105">[102]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Strabo">Strabo</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rosso_Fiorentino_-_Moses_defending_the_Daughters_of_Jethro_-_Web_Gallery_of_Art.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1140" data-file-width="831" decoding="async" height="302" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Rosso_Fiorentino_-_Moses_defending_the_Daughters_of_Jethro_-_Web_Gallery_of_Art.jpg/220px-Rosso_Fiorentino_-_Moses_defending_the_Daughters_of_Jethro_-_Web_Gallery_of_Art.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Rosso_Fiorentino_-_Moses_defending_the_Daughters_of_Jethro_-_Web_Gallery_of_Art.jpg/330px-Rosso_Fiorentino_-_Moses_defending_the_Daughters_of_Jethro_-_Web_Gallery_of_Art.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Rosso_Fiorentino_-_Moses_defending_the_Daughters_of_Jethro_-_Web_Gallery_of_Art.jpg/440px-Rosso_Fiorentino_-_Moses_defending_the_Daughters_of_Jethro_-_Web_Gallery_of_Art.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rosso_Fiorentino_-_Moses_defending_the_Daughters_of_Jethro_-_Web_Gallery_of_Art.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Moses_Defends_Jethro%27s_Daughters" title="Moses Defends Jethro's Daughters">Moses Defends Jethro's Daughters</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Rosso_Fiorentino" title="Rosso Fiorentino">Rosso Fiorentino</a>, c.1523-1524</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Strabo" title="Strabo">Strabo</a>, a Greek historian, geographer and philosopher, in his <i><a href="/wiki/Geographica" title="Geographica">Geographica</a></i> (c.Â 24Â CE), wrote in detail about Moses, whom he considered to be an Egyptian who deplored the situation in his homeland, and thereby attracted many followers who respected the deity. He writes, for example, that Moses opposed the picturing of the deity in the form of man or animal, and was convinced that the deity was an entity which encompassed everything â land and sea:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShmuel19761132_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShmuel19761132-106">[103]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>35. An Egyptian priest named Moses, who possessed a portion of the country called the <a href="/wiki/Lower_Egypt" title="Lower Egypt">Lower Egypt</a>, being dissatisfied with the established institutions there, left it and came to Judaea with a large body of people who worshipped the Divinity. He declared and taught that the Egyptians and Africans entertained erroneous sentiments, in representing the Divinity under the likeness of wild beasts and cattle of the field; that the <a href="/wiki/Greeks" title="Greeks">Greeks</a> also were in error in making images of their gods after the human form. For God [said he] may be this one thing which encompasses us all, land and sea, which we call heaven, or the universe, or the nature of things....
</p><p>
36. By such doctrine Moses persuaded a large body of right-minded persons to accompany him to the place where <a href="/wiki/Jerusalem" title="Jerusalem">Jerusalem</a> now stands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Strabo_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-Strabo-107">[104]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>In Strabo's writings of the history of <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">Judaism</a> as he understood it, he describes various stages in its development: from the first stage, including Moses and his direct heirs; to the final stage where "the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Temple_of_Jerusalem" title="Temple of Jerusalem">Temple of Jerusalem</a> continued to be surrounded by an aura of sanctity". Strabo's "positive and unequivocal appreciation of Moses' personality is among the most sympathetic in all ancient literature."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShmuel19761133_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShmuel19761133-108">[105]</a></sup> His portrayal of Moses is said to be similar to the writing of <a href="/wiki/Hecataeus_of_Abdera" title="Hecataeus of Abdera">Hecataeus</a> who "described Moses as a man who excelled in wisdom and courage".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShmuel19761133_108-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShmuel19761133-108">[105]</a></sup>
</p><p>Egyptologist <a href="/wiki/Jan_Assmann" title="Jan Assmann">Jan Assmann</a> concludes that Strabo was the historian "who came closest to a construction of Moses' religion as <a href="/wiki/Monotheism" title="Monotheism">monotheistic</a> and as a pronounced counter-religion." It recognized "only one divine being whom no image can representÂ ... [and] the only way to approach this god is to live in virtue and in justice."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAssmann199738_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAssmann199738-109">[106]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tacitus">Tacitus</span></h3>
<p>The Roman historian <a href="/wiki/Tacitus" title="Tacitus">Tacitus</a> (c. 56â120Â CE) refers to Moses by noting that the Jewish religion was monotheistic and without a clear image. His primary work, wherein he describes <a href="/wiki/Jewish_philosophy" title="Jewish philosophy">Jewish philosophy</a>, is his <i><a href="/wiki/Histories_(Tacitus)" title="Histories (Tacitus)">Histories</a></i> (c. 100), where, according to 18th-century translator and Irish dramatist <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Murphy_(writer)" title="Arthur Murphy (writer)">Arthur Murphy</a>, as a result of the Jewish worship of one God, "<a href="/wiki/Paganism" title="Paganism">pagan</a> mythology fell into contempt".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[107]</a></sup> Tacitus states that, despite various opinions current in his day regarding the Jews' ethnicity, most of his sources are in agreement that there was an Exodus from Egypt. By his account, the Pharaoh <a href="/wiki/Bakenranef" title="Bakenranef">Bocchoris</a>, suffering from a <a href="/wiki/Plague_(disease)" title="Plague (disease)">plague</a>, banished the Jews in response to an oracle of the god <a href="/wiki/Zeus" title="Zeus">Zeus</a>-<a href="/wiki/Amun" title="Amun">Amun</a>.
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p> A motley crowd was thus collected and abandoned in the desert. While all the other outcasts lay idly lamenting, one of them, named Moses, advised them not to look for help to gods or men, since both had deserted them, but to trust rather in themselves, and accept as divine the guidance of the first being, by whose aid they should get out of their present plight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tacitus_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tacitus-111">[108]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>In this version, Moses and the Jews wander through the desert for only six days, capturing the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Land" title="Holy Land">Holy Land</a> on the seventh.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tacitus_111-1"><a href="#cite_note-Tacitus-111">[108]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Longinus">Longinus</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Brazen_Serpent.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="388" data-file-width="301" decoding="async" height="284" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/The_Brazen_Serpent.jpg/220px-The_Brazen_Serpent.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/af/The_Brazen_Serpent.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Brazen_Serpent.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Moses lifts up the <a href="/wiki/Nehushtan" title="Nehushtan">brass serpent</a>, curing the Israelites from poisonous snake bites in a painting by <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_West" title="Benjamin West">Benjamin West</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Septuagint" title="Septuagint">Septuagint</a>, the Greek version of the Hebrew Bible, impressed the pagan author of the famous classical book of literary criticism, <i><a href="/wiki/On_the_Sublime" title="On the Sublime">On the Sublime</a></i>, traditionally attributed to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Longinus_(literature)" title="Longinus (literature)">Longinus</a>. The date of composition is unknown, but it is commonly assigned to the late 1st century C.E.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[109]</a></sup>
</p><p>The writer quotes <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Genesis" title="Book of Genesis">Genesis</a> in a "style which presents the nature of the deity in a manner suitable to his pure and great being," however he does not mention Moses by name, calling him 'no chance person' (<span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">Î¿á½Ï á½ ÏÏÏá½¼Î½ á¼Î½Î®Ï</span></span>) but "the Lawgiver" (<span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">Î¸ÎµÏÎ¼Î¿Î¸Î­ÏÎ·Ï</span></span>, <a href="/wiki/Archon#Ancient_Greece" title="Archon">thesmothete</a>) of the Jews," a term that puts him on a par with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lycurgus_of_Sparta" title="Lycurgus of Sparta">Lycurgus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Minos" title="Minos">Minos</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[110]</a></sup> Aside from a reference to <a href="/wiki/Cicero" title="Cicero">Cicero</a>, Moses is the only non-Greek writer quoted in the work, contextually he is put on a par with <a href="/wiki/Homer" title="Homer">Homer</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFeldman1998133_105-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFeldman1998133-105">[102]</a></sup> and he is described "with far more admiration than even Greek writers who treated Moses with respect, such as <a href="/wiki/Hecataeus_of_Abdera" title="Hecataeus of Abdera">Hecataeus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Strabo" title="Strabo">Strabo</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShmuel19761140_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShmuel19761140-114">[111]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Josephus">Josephus</span></h3>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Josephus" title="Josephus">Josephus</a>' (37 â c.Â 100 CE) <i>Antiquities of the Jews</i>, Moses is mentioned throughout. For example Book VIII Ch. IV, describes <a href="/wiki/Solomon%27s_Temple" title="Solomon's Temple">Solomon's Temple</a>, also known as the First Temple, at the time the <a href="/wiki/Ark_of_the_Covenant" title="Ark of the Covenant">Ark of the Covenant</a> was first moved into the newly built temple:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>When <a href="/wiki/Solomon" title="Solomon">King Solomon</a> had finished these works, these large and beautiful buildings, and had laid up his donations in the temple, and all this in the interval of seven years, and had given a demonstration of his riches and alacrity therein; ... he also wrote to the rulers and elders of the Hebrews, and ordered all the people to gather themselves together to Jerusalem, both to see the temple which he had built, and to remove the ark of God into it; and when this invitation of the whole body of the people to come to Jerusalem was everywhere carried abroad, ... The <a href="/wiki/Sukkot" title="Sukkot">Feast of Tabernacles</a> happened to fall at the same time, which was kept by the Hebrews as a most holy and most eminent feast. So they carried the ark and the tabernacle which Moses had pitched, and all the vessels that were for ministration to the sacrifices of God, and removed them to the temple. ... Now the ark contained nothing else but those two tables of stone that preserved the ten commandments, which God spake to Moses in Mount Sinai, and which were engraved upon themÂ ...<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[112]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>According to Feldman, Josephus also attaches particular significance to Moses's possession of the "cardinal virtues of wisdom, courage, temperance, and justice". He also includes piety as an added fifth virtue. In addition, he "stresses Moses' willingness to undergo toil and his careful avoidance of bribery. Like <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Philosopher_king" title="Philosopher king">philosopher-king</a>, Moses excels as an educator."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFeldman1998130_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFeldman1998130-116">[113]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Numenius">Numenius</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Numenius_of_Apamea" title="Numenius of Apamea">Numenius</a>, a Greek philosopher who was a native of <a href="/wiki/Apamea,_Syria" title="Apamea, Syria">Apamea</a>, in Syria, wrote during the latter half of the 2nd century CE. Historian Kennieth Guthrie writes that "Numenius is perhaps the only recognized Greek philosopher who explicitly studied Moses, the prophets, and the life of <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">Jesus</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuthrie1917194_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuthrie1917194-117">[114]</a></sup> He describes his background:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Numenius was a man of the world; he was not limited to <a href="/wiki/Greco-Roman_mysteries" title="Greco-Roman mysteries">Greek and Egyptian mysteries</a>, but talked familiarly of the myths of <a href="/wiki/Brahmin" title="Brahmin">Brahmins</a> and <a href="/wiki/Magi" title="Magi">Magi</a>.  It is however his knowledge and use of the Hebrew scriptures which distinguished him from other Greek philosophers. He refers to Moses simply as "the prophet", exactly as for him Homer is the poet. Plato is described as a Greek Moses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuthrie1917101_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuthrie1917101-118">[115]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Justin_Martyr">Justin Martyr</span></h3>
<p>The Christian saint and religious philosopher <a href="/wiki/Justin_Martyr" title="Justin Martyr">Justin Martyr</a> (103â165 CE) drew the same conclusion as <a href="/wiki/Numenius_of_Apamea" title="Numenius of Apamea">Numenius</a>, according to other experts. Theologian Paul Blackham notes that Justin considered Moses to be "more trustworthy, profound and truthful because he is <i>older</i> than the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_philosophy" title="Ancient Greek philosophy">Greek philosophers</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlackham200539_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlackham200539-119">[116]</a></sup> He quotes him:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>I will begin, then, with our first prophet and lawgiver, MosesÂ ... that you may know that, of all your teachers, whether sages, poets, historians, philosophers, or lawgivers, by far the oldest, as the Greek histories show us, was Moses, who was our first religious teacher.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlackham200539_119-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlackham200539-119">[116]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Abrahamic_religions">Abrahamic religions</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1066479718" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="infobox vcard"><tbody><tr><th class="infobox-above n" colspan="2" style="background-color:gold;"><div class="fn" style="display:inline;">Prophet Moses</div></th></tr><tr><td class="infobox-image" colspan="2"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MosesStrikingTheRock_GREBBER.jpg"><img alt="MosesStrikingTheRock GREBBER.jpg" data-file-height="1168" data-file-width="917" decoding="async" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/MosesStrikingTheRock_GREBBER.jpg/230px-MosesStrikingTheRock_GREBBER.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/MosesStrikingTheRock_GREBBER.jpg/345px-MosesStrikingTheRock_GREBBER.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/MosesStrikingTheRock_GREBBER.jpg/460px-MosesStrikingTheRock_GREBBER.jpg 2x" width="230"/></a><div class="infobox-caption">Moses striking the rock, 1630 by <a href="/wiki/Pieter_de_Grebber" title="Pieter de Grebber">Pieter de Grebber</a></div></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-header" colspan="2" style="background-color:gold;">Prophet, Saint, Seer, Lawgiver, Apostle to Pharaoh, Reformer, God-seer</th></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Born</th><td class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/Land_of_Goshen" title="Land of Goshen">Goshen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lower_Egypt" title="Lower Egypt">Lower Egypt</a></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Died</th><td class="infobox-data"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mount_Nebo_(Jordan)" title="Mount Nebo (Jordan)">Mount Nebo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Moab" title="Moab">Moab</a></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">VeneratedÂ in</th><td class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">Judaism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>, <a class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dana_2008_17_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dana_2008_17-7">[6]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bah%C3%A1%CA%BC%C3%AD_Faith" title="BahÃ¡Ê¼Ã­ Faith">BahÃ¡Ê¼Ã­
