and April 14</td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Saint_symbolism" title="Saint symbolism">Attributes</a></th><td class="infobox-data">Tablets of the Law</td></tr></tbody></table>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Judaism">Judaism</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Moses_in_rabbinic_literature" title="Moses in rabbinic literature">Moses in rabbinic literature</a></div>
<p>Most of what is known about Moses from the Bible comes from the books of <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Exodus" title="Book of Exodus">Exodus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Leviticus" title="Book of Leviticus">Leviticus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Numbers" title="Book of Numbers">Numbers</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Deuteronomy" title="Book of Deuteronomy">Deuteronomy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVan_Seters2004194_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVan_Seters2004194-120">[117]</a></sup> The majority of scholars consider the compilation of these books to go back to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yehud_Medinata" title="Yehud Medinata">Persian period</a>, 538â332Â BCE, but based on earlier written and oral traditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[119]</a></sup> There is a wealth of stories and additional information about Moses in the <a href="/wiki/Jewish_apocrypha" title="Jewish apocrypha">Jewish apocrypha</a> and in the genre of <a href="/wiki/Rabbi" title="Rabbi">rabbinical</a> <a href="/wiki/Exegesis" title="Exegesis">exegesis</a> known as <a href="/wiki/Midrash" title="Midrash">Midrash</a>, as well as in the primary works of the Jewish <a href="/wiki/Oral_law" title="Oral law">oral law</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Mishnah" title="Mishnah">Mishnah</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Talmud" title="Talmud">Talmud</a>. Moses is also given a number of bynames in Jewish tradition. The <a href="/wiki/Midrash" title="Midrash">Midrash</a> identifies Moses as one of seven biblical personalities who were called by various names.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[120]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (May 2022)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup>} Moses's other names were Jekuthiel (by his mother), Heber (by <a href="/wiki/Amram" title="Amram">his father</a>), Jered (by <a href="/wiki/Miriam" title="Miriam">Miriam</a>), Avi Zanoah (by Aaron), <a href="/wiki/Avigdor_(name)" title="Avigdor (name)">Avi Gedor</a> (by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kohath" title="Kohath">Kohath</a>), Avi Soco (by his wet-nurse), Shemaiah ben Nethanel (by people of Israel).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[121]</a></sup> Moses is also attributed the names Toviah (as a first name), and Levi (as a family name) (Vayikra Rabbah 1:3), Heman,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[122]</a></sup> Mechoqeiq (lawgiver),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[123]</a></sup> and Ehl Gav Ish (Numbers 12:3).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[124]</a></sup> In another <a href="/wiki/Exegesis" title="Exegesis">exegesis</a>, Moses had ascended to the first heaven until the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seven_Heavens" title="Seven Heavens">seventh</a>, even visited <a href="/wiki/Paradise" title="Paradise">Paradise</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hell" title="Hell">Hell</a> alive, after he saw the <a href="/wiki/Burning_bush" title="Burning bush">divine vision</a> in Mount Horeb.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ginzberg_1909_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ginzberg_1909-128">[125]</a></sup>
</p><p>Jewish historians who lived at <a href="/wiki/Alexandria" title="Alexandria">Alexandria</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Eupolemus" title="Eupolemus">Eupolemus</a>, attributed to Moses the feat of having taught the <a href="/wiki/Phoenicia" title="Phoenicia">Phoenicians</a> <a href="/wiki/Phoenician_alphabet" title="Phoenician alphabet">their alphabet</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[126]</a></sup> similar to legends of <a href="/wiki/Thoth" title="Thoth">Thoth</a>. <a href="/wiki/Artapanus_of_Alexandria" title="Artapanus of Alexandria">Artapanus of Alexandria</a> explicitly identified Moses not only with Thoth/<a href="/wiki/Hermes" title="Hermes">Hermes</a>, but also with the Greek figure <a href="/wiki/Musaeus_of_Athens" title="Musaeus of Athens">Musaeus</a> (whom he called "the teacher of <a href="/wiki/Orpheus" title="Orpheus">Orpheus</a>"), and ascribed to him the division of Egypt into 36 districts, each with its own liturgy. He named the princess who adopted Moses as Merris, wife of Pharaoh Chenephres.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[127]</a></sup>
</p><p>Jewish tradition considers Moses to be the greatest prophet who ever lived.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ginzberg_1909_128-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ginzberg_1909-128">[125]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[128]</a></sup> Despite his importance, Judaism stresses that Moses was a human being, and is therefore not to be worshipped.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Only God is worthy of worship in Judaism.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>To <a href="/wiki/Orthodox_Judaism" title="Orthodox Judaism">Orthodox Jews</a>, Moses is called <i>Moshe Rabbenu, 'Eved HaShem, Avi haNeviim zya"a</i>: "Our Leader Moshe, Servant of God, Father of all the Prophets (may his merit shield us, amen)". In the orthodox view, Moses received not only the Torah, but also the revealed (written and oral) and the hidden (the <i>'hokhmat nistar</i> teachings, which gave Judaism the <a href="/wiki/Zohar" title="Zohar">Zohar</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Shimon_bar_Yochai" title="Shimon bar Yochai">Rashbi</a>, the Torah of the <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Luria" title="Isaac Luria">Ari haQadosh</a> and all that is discussed in the Heavenly Yeshiva between the <a href="/wiki/Moshe_Chaim_Luzzatto" title="Moshe Chaim Luzzatto">Ramhal</a> and his masters).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Arising in part from his age of death (120 years, according to Deut. 34:7) and that "his eye had not dimmed, and his vigor had not diminished", the phrase "<a href="/wiki/Live_until_120" title="Live until 120">may you live to 120</a>" has become a common blessing among Jews (120 is stated as the maximum age for all of <a href="/wiki/Noah" title="Noah">Noah</a>'s descendants in Genesis 6:3).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Christianity">Christianity</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The-Transfiguration-1480-xx-Giovanni-Bellini.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1444" data-file-width="1914" decoding="async" height="189" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/The-Transfiguration-1480-xx-Giovanni-Bellini.JPG/250px-The-Transfiguration-1480-xx-Giovanni-Bellini.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/The-Transfiguration-1480-xx-Giovanni-Bellini.JPG/375px-The-Transfiguration-1480-xx-Giovanni-Bellini.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/The-Transfiguration-1480-xx-Giovanni-Bellini.JPG/500px-The-Transfiguration-1480-xx-Giovanni-Bellini.JPG 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The-Transfiguration-1480-xx-Giovanni-Bellini.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Transfiguration_of_Christ_(Bellini)" title="Transfiguration of Christ (Bellini)">Moses, to the left of Jesus</a>, at the <a href="/wiki/Transfiguration_of_Jesus" title="Transfiguration of Jesus">Transfiguration of Jesus</a>, by <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Bellini" title="Giovanni Bellini">Giovanni Bellini</a>, c. 1480</div></div></div>
<p>Moses is mentioned more often in the <a href="/wiki/New_Testament" title="New Testament">New Testament</a> than any other <a href="/wiki/Old_Testament" title="Old Testament">Old Testament</a> figure. For <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christians</a>, Moses is often a symbol of <a href="/wiki/Divine_law" title="Divine law">God's law</a>, as reinforced and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Expounding_of_the_Law" title="Expounding of the Law">expounded on</a> in the teachings of <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">Jesus</a>. New Testament writers often compared Jesus's words and deeds with Moses's to explain Jesus's mission. In <a href="/wiki/Acts_of_the_Apostles" title="Acts of the Apostles">Acts</a> 7:39â43, 51â53, for example, the rejection of Moses by the Jews who worshipped the <a href="/wiki/Golden_calf" title="Golden calf">golden calf</a> is likened to the rejection of Jesus by the Jews that continued in traditional Judaism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[129]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[130]</a></sup>
</p><p>Moses also figures in several of Jesus's messages. When he met the <a href="/wiki/Pharisees" title="Pharisees">Pharisee</a> <a href="/wiki/Nicodemus" title="Nicodemus">Nicodemus</a> at night in the third chapter of the <a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_John" title="Gospel of John">Gospel of John</a>, he compared Moses's lifting up of the <a href="/wiki/Nehushtan" title="Nehushtan">bronze serpent</a> in the wilderness, which any Israelite could look at and be healed, to his own lifting up (by his death and <a href="/wiki/Resurrection" title="Resurrection">resurrection</a>) for the people to look at and be healed. In the sixth chapter, Jesus responded to the people's claim that Moses provided them <i><a href="/wiki/Manna" title="Manna">manna</a></i> in the wilderness by saying that it was not Moses, but God, who provided. Calling himself the "<a href="/wiki/Bread_of_Life_Discourse" title="Bread of Life Discourse">bread of life</a>", Jesus stated that he was provided to feed God's people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[131]</a></sup>
</p><p>Moses, along with <a href="/wiki/Elijah" title="Elijah">Elijah</a>, is presented as meeting with Jesus in all three <a href="/wiki/Synoptic_Gospels" title="Synoptic Gospels">Synoptic Gospels</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Transfiguration_of_Jesus" title="Transfiguration of Jesus">Transfiguration of Jesus</a> in <a href="/wiki/Matthew_17" title="Matthew 17">Matthew 17</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mark_9" title="Mark 9">Mark 9</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Luke_9" title="Luke 9">Luke 9</a>, respectively. In <a href="/wiki/Matthew_23" title="Matthew 23">Matthew 23</a>, in what is the first attested use of a phrase referring to this rabbinical usage (the Graeco-Aramaic <span title="Hebrew-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="he">×§×ª××¨× ×××©×</span></span>), Jesus refers to the scribes and the Pharisees, in a passage critical of them, as having seated themselves "on the chair of Moses" (<a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>: <span lang="el">á¼Ïá½¶ Ïá¿Ï ÎÏÏÏÎ­ÏÏ ÎºÎ±Î¸Î­Î´ÏÎ±Ï</span>, <i>epÃ¬ tÄs MÅÃ¼sÃ©Ås kathÃ©dras</i>) <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[132]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tomson2019_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tomson2019-136">[133]</a></sup>
</p><p>His relevance to modern Christianity has not diminished. Moses is considered to be a <a href="/wiki/Saint" title="Saint">saint</a> by several churches; and is commemorated as a prophet in the respective <a href="/wiki/Calendar_of_saints" title="Calendar of saints">Calendars of Saints</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">Eastern Orthodox Church</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Roman Catholic Church</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Lutheranism" title="Lutheranism">Lutheran</a> churches on September 4. In <a href="/wiki/September_4_(Eastern_Orthodox_liturgics)" title="September 4 (Eastern Orthodox liturgics)">Eastern Orthodox liturgics</a> for September 4, Moses is commemorated as the "Holy Prophet and God-seer Moses, on Mount Nebo".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[134]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[135]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[note 4]</a></sup> The Orthodox Church also commemorates him on the <a href="/wiki/Nativity_Fast#Sunday_of_the_Forefathers" title="Nativity Fast">Sunday of the Forefathers</a>, two Sundays before the <a href="/wiki/Nativity_of_Jesus" title="Nativity of Jesus">Nativity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[137]</a></sup> Moses is also commemorated on July 20 with <a href="/wiki/Aaron" title="Aaron">Aaron</a>, <a href="/wiki/Elijah" title="Elijah">Elias</a> and <a href="/wiki/Elisha" title="Elisha">Eliseus</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[138]</a></sup> and on April 14 with all saint <a href="/wiki/Sinaia_Monastery" title="Sinaia Monastery">Sinai</a> monks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[139]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Armenian_Apostolic_Church" title="Armenian Apostolic Church">Armenian Apostolic Church</a> commemorates him as one of the Holy Forefathers in their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Calendar_of_Saints_(Armenian_Apostolic_Church)" title="Calendar of Saints (Armenian Apostolic Church)">Calendar of Saints</a> on July 30.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[140]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Catholicism">Catholicism</span></h4>
<p>In Catholicism Moses is seen as a type of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jesus_Christ" title="Jesus Christ">Jesus Christ</a>. <a href="/wiki/Justus_Knecht" title="Justus Knecht">Justus Knecht</a> writes:
</p>
<blockquote><p>Through Moses God instituted the Old Law, on which account he is called the mediator of the Old Law. As such, Moses was a striking type of Jesus Christ, who instituted the New Law. Moses, as a child, was condemned to death by a <a href="/wiki/Herod_the_Great" title="Herod the Great">cruel king</a>, and was saved in a wonderful way; Jesus Christ was condemned by Herod, and also wonderfully saved. Moses forsook the kingâs court so as to help his persecuted brethren; the Son of God left the glory of heaven to save us sinners. Moses prepared himself in the desert for his vocation, freed his people from slavery, and proved his divine mission by great miracles; Jesus Christ proved by still greater miracles that He was the only begotten Son of God. Moses was the advocate of his people; Jesus was our advocate with His Father on the Cross, and is eternally so in heaven. Moses was the law-giver of his people and announced to them the word of God: Jesus Christ is the supreme law-giver, and not only announced God's word, but is Himself the Eternal Word made flesh. Moses was the leader of the people to the <a href="/wiki/Promised_Land" title="Promised Land">Promised Land</a>: Jesus is our leader on our journey to heaven.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[141]</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Mormonism">Mormonism</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Moses" title="Book of Moses">Book of Moses</a></div>
<p>Members of <a href="/wiki/The_Church_of_Jesus_Christ_of_Latter-day_Saints" title="The Church of Jesus Christ of Latter-day Saints">The Church of Jesus Christ of Latter-day Saints</a> (colloquially called <a href="/wiki/Mormons" title="Mormons">Mormons</a>) generally view Moses in the same way that other Christians do. However, in addition to accepting the biblical account of Moses, Mormons include <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Moses" title="Book of Moses">Selections from the Book of Moses</a> as part of their scriptural canon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[142]</a></sup> This book is believed to be the translated writings of Moses, and is included in the <a href="/wiki/Pearl_of_Great_Price_(Mormonism)" title="Pearl of Great Price (Mormonism)">Pearl of Great Price</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[143]</a></sup>
</p><p>Latter-day Saints are also unique in believing that Moses was taken to heaven without having tasted death (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Translation_(LDS_Church)" title="Translation (LDS Church)">translated</a>). In addition, <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Smith" title="Joseph Smith">Joseph Smith</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Cowdery" title="Oliver Cowdery">Oliver Cowdery</a> stated that on April 3, 1836, Moses appeared to them in the <a href="/wiki/Kirtland_Temple" title="Kirtland Temple">Kirtland Temple</a> (located in <a href="/wiki/Kirtland,_Ohio" title="Kirtland, Ohio">Kirtland, Ohio</a>) in a glorified, immortal, physical form and bestowed upon them the "keys of the gathering of Israel from the four parts of the earth, and the leading of the <a href="/wiki/Ten_Lost_Tribes#Latter-day_Saint_Movement" title="Ten Lost Tribes">ten tribes</a> from the land of the north".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[144]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Islam">Islam</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Moses_in_Islam" title="Moses in Islam">Moses in Islam</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biblical_narratives_and_the_Qur%27an#Moses_(MÅ«sÄ_ÙÙØ³Ù)" title="Biblical narratives and the Qur'an">Biblical narratives and the Qur'an Â§Â Moses (MÅ«sÄ ÙÙØ³Ù)</a></div>

him</a></div></td></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-content" style="hlist">
<div class="sidebar-list mw-collapsible mw-collapsed"><div class="sidebar-list-title" style="background-color:#dcf5dc;;background:#dcf5dc;">Revelations</div><div class="sidebar-list-content mw-collapsible-content" style="padding:2px 0px 0px 0px;">

</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-content" style="hlist">
<div class="sidebar-list mw-collapsible mw-collapsed"><div class="sidebar-list-title" style="background-color:#dcf5dc;;background:#dcf5dc;">Related articles</div><div class="sidebar-list-content mw-collapsible-content">

<li><a class="mw-selflink selflink">Moses</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ulu%27l_azm_prophets" title="Ulu'l azm prophets">Ulu'l azm prophets</a></li></ul></div></div></td>
</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-below">
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Category:Prophecy_in_Islam" title="Category:Prophecy in Islam">Category:Prophecy in Islam</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Portal:Islam" title="Portal:Islam">Islam portal</a></li></ul></td></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar" style="background:white; padding:0.5px"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1063604349">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar a>span,.mw-parser-output .navbar a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Musa" title="Template:Musa"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Musa" title="Template talk:Musa"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Musa&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Moses is mentioned more in the <a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Quran</a> than any other individual and his life is narrated and recounted more than that of any other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prophets_of_Islam" title="Prophets of Islam">Islamic prophet</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKeeler200555â66_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKeeler200555â66-149">[145]</a></sup> Islamically, Moses is described in ways which parallel the Islamic prophet <a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">Muhammad</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKeeler200555â56_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKeeler200555â56-150">[146]</a></sup> Like Muhammad, Moses is defined in the Quran as both prophet (<i>nabi</i>) and messenger (<i><a href="/wiki/Prophets_and_messengers_in_Islam" title="Prophets and messengers in Islam">rasul</a></i>), the latter term indicating that he was one of those prophets who brought a scripture and law to his people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[147]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKeeler200555_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKeeler200555-152">[148]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nabi_Musa_jerico-Jerusalam.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1944" data-file-width="2592" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Nabi_Musa_jerico-Jerusalam.jpg/220px-Nabi_Musa_jerico-Jerusalam.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Nabi_Musa_jerico-Jerusalam.jpg/330px-Nabi_Musa_jerico-Jerusalam.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Nabi_Musa_jerico-Jerusalam.jpg/440px-Nabi_Musa_jerico-Jerusalam.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nabi_Musa_jerico-Jerusalam.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Nabi_Musa" title="Nabi Musa">Maqam El-Nabi Musa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jericho" title="Jericho">Jericho</a></div></div></div>
<p>Moses is mentioned <a href="/wiki/Moses_in_Islam#Quranic_references" title="Moses in Islam">502 times in the Quran</a>. Most of the key events in Moses's life which are narrated in the Bible are to be found dispersed through the different chapters (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Suwar" title="Suwar">suwar</a></i>) of the Quran, with a story about meeting <a href="/wiki/Khidr" title="Khidr">Khidr</a> which is not found in the Bible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKeeler200555â66_149-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKeeler200555â66-149">[145]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the Moses story related by the Quran, Jochebed is commanded by God to place Moses in an ark and cast him on the waters of the Nile, thus abandoning him completely to God's protection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKeeler200555â66_149-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKeeler200555â66-149">[145]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[149]</a></sup> The Pharaoh's wife <a href="/wiki/Asiya" title="Asiya">Asiya</a>, not his daughter, found Moses floating in the waters of the Nile. She convinced the Pharaoh to keep him as their son because they were not blessed with any children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[150]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[151]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[152]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Quran's account has emphasized Moses's mission to invite the Pharaoh to accept God's divine message<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[153]</a></sup> as well as give salvation to the Israelites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKeeler200555â66_149-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKeeler200555â66-149">[145]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[154]</a></sup> According to the Quran, Moses encourages the Israelites to enter Canaan, but they are unwilling to fight the Canaanites, fearing certain defeat. Moses responds by pleading to Allah that he and his brother Aaron be separated from the rebellious Israelites, after which the Israelites are made to wander for 40 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[155]</a></sup>
</p><p>One of the <a href="/wiki/Hadith" title="Hadith">hadith</a>, or traditional narratives about Muhammad's life, describes a meeting in heaven between Moses and Muhammad, which resulted in Muslims observing <a href="/wiki/Salah" title="Salah">5 daily prayers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[156]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Huston_Smith" title="Huston Smith">Huston Smith</a> says this was "one of the crucial events in Muhammad's life".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[157]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to some Islamic tradition, Moses is believed to be buried at <a href="/wiki/Nabi_Musa" title="Nabi Musa">Maqam El-Nabi Musa</a>, near <a href="/wiki/Jericho" title="Jericho">Jericho</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[158]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Bah.C3.A1.CA.BC.C3.AD_Faith"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="BahÃ¡Ê¼Ã­_Faith">BahÃ¡Ê¼Ã­ Faith</span></h3>
<p>Moses is one of the most important of God's messengers in the <a href="/wiki/Bah%C3%A1%CA%BC%C3%AD_Faith" title="BahÃ¡Ê¼Ã­ Faith">BahÃ¡Ê¼Ã­ Faith</a>, being designated a <a href="/wiki/Manifestation_of_God_(Bah%C3%A1%CA%BC%C3%AD_Faith)" title="Manifestation of God (BahÃ¡Ê¼Ã­ Faith)">Manifestation of God</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[159]</a></sup> An epithet of Moses in BahÃ¡Ê¼Ã­ scriptures is the "One Who Conversed with God".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[160]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to the BahÃ¡Ê¼Ã­ Faith, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bah%C3%A1%27u%27ll%C3%A1h" title="BahÃ¡'u'llÃ¡h">BahÃ¡'u'llÃ¡h</a>, the founder of the faith, is the one who spoke to Moses from the <a href="/wiki/Burning_bush" title="Burning bush">burning bush</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[161]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/%CA%BBAbdu%27l-Bah%C3%A1" title="Ê»Abdu'l-BahÃ¡">Ê»Abdu'l-BahÃ¡</a> has highlighted the fact that Moses, like <a href="/wiki/Abraham" title="Abraham">Abraham</a>, had none of the makings of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_Man_theory" title="Great Man theory">great man of history</a>, but through God's assistance he was able to achieve many great things. He is described as having been "for a long time a shepherd in the wilderness", of having had a <a href="/wiki/Stuttering" title="Stuttering">stammer</a>, and of being "much hated and detested" by Pharaoh and the ancient Egyptians of his time. He is said to have been raised in an oppressive household, and to have been known, in Egypt, as a man who had committed murder â though he had done so in order to prevent an act of cruelty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[162]</a></sup>
</p><p>Nevertheless, like Abraham, through the assistance of God, he achieved great things and gained renown even beyond the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a>. Chief among these achievements was the freeing of his people, the Hebrews, from bondage in Egypt and leading "them to the Holy Land". He is viewed as the one who bestowed on Israel "the religious and the civil law" which gave them "honour among all nations", and which spread their fame to different parts of the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clifford_1937_14â15_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-Clifford_1937_14â15-167">[163]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag failed verification of its source citation(s). (June 2019)">failed verification</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Furthermore, through the law, Moses is believed to have led the Hebrews "to the highest possible degree of <a href="/wiki/Civilization" title="Civilization">civilization</a> at that period". Abdulâl-Baha asserts that the ancient Greek philosophers regarded "the illustrious men of Israel as models of perfection".<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag failed verification of its source citation(s). (June 2019)">failed verification</span></a></i>]</sup> Chief among these philosophers, he says, was <a href="/wiki/Socrates" title="Socrates">Socrates</a> who "visited Syria, and took from the children of Israel the teachings of the Unity of God and of the immortality of the soul".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clifford_1937_14â15_167-1"><a href="#cite_note-Clifford_1937_14â15-167">[163]</a></sup>
</p><p>Moses is further seen as paving the way for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bah%C3%A1%27u%27ll%C3%A1h" title="BahÃ¡'u'llÃ¡h">BahÃ¡'u'llÃ¡h</a> and his ultimate revelation, and as a teacher of truth, whose teachings were in line with the customs of his time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[164]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Druze_faith">Druze faith</span></h3>
<p>Moses is considered an important prophet of God in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Druze_faith" title="Druze faith">Druze faith</a>, being among the seven prophets who appeared in different periods of history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hitti_1928_37_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-Hitti_1928_37-6">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dana_2008_17_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-Dana_2008_17-7">[6]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy_in_politics_and_law">Legacy in politics and law</span></h2>
<div class="boilerplate metadata plainlinks tfd" id="tfd" style="background-color: transparent; padding: 0; font-size:xx-small; color:#000000; text-align: center; border-bottom:1px solid #AAAAAA; overflow:hidden;">â¹Â The <a href="/wiki/Help:Template" title="Help:Template">template</a> below (<i><a href="/wiki/Template:Globalize" title="Template:Globalize">Globalize</a></i>) is being considered for merging. See <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Templates_for_discussion/Log/2022_October_6#Template:Globalize" title="Wikipedia:Templates for discussion/Log/2022 October 6">templates for discussion</a> to help reach a data-file-height="290" data-file-width="350" decoding="async" height="40" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Ambox_globe_content.svg/48px-Ambox_globe_content.svg.png" <div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Moses_LOC.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="501" data-file-width="317" decoding="async" height="348" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Moses_LOC.jpg/220px-Moses_LOC.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/5f/Moses_LOC.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Moses_LOC.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Statue of Moses at the <a href="/wiki/Library_of_Congress" title="Library of Congress">Library of Congress</a></div></div></div>
<p>In a metaphorical sense in the Christian tradition, a "Moses" has been referred to as the leader who delivers the people from a terrible situation. Among the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_Presidents_of_the_United_States" title="List of Presidents of the United States">Presidents of the United States</a> known to have used the symbolism of Moses were <a href="/wiki/Harry_S._Truman" title="Harry S. Truman">Harry S. Truman</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Carter" title="Jimmy Carter">Jimmy Carter</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Reagan" title="Ronald Reagan">Ronald Reagan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bill_Clinton" title="Bill Clinton">Bill Clinton</a>, <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">George W. Bush</a> and <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a>, who referred to his supporters as "the Moses generation".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[165]</a></sup>
</p><p>In subsequent years, theologians linked the Ten Commandments with the formation of early <a href="/wiki/Democracy" title="Democracy">democracy</a>. Scottish theologian <a href="/wiki/William_Barclay_(theologian)" title="William Barclay (theologian)">William Barclay</a> described them as "the universal foundation of all thingsÂ ... the law without which <a href="/wiki/Nation" title="Nation">nationhood</a> is impossible. ... Our society is founded upon it."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[166]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pope_Francis" title="Pope Francis">Pope Francis</a> addressed the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">United States Congress</a> in 2015 stating that all people need to "keep alive their sense of unity by means of just legislationÂ ... [and] the figure of Moses leads us directly to God and thus to the transcendent dignity of the human being".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[167]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_United_States_history">In United States history</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Pilgrims">Pilgrims</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Embarkation_of_the_Pilgrims.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1964" data-file-width="3000" decoding="async" height="196" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Embarkation_of_the_Pilgrims.jpg/300px-Embarkation_of_the_Pilgrims.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Embarkation_of_the_Pilgrims.jpg/450px-Embarkation_of_the_Pilgrims.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Embarkation_of_the_Pilgrims.jpg/600px-Embarkation_of_the_Pilgrims.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Embarkation_of_the_Pilgrims.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Pilgrims <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_Carver_(Mayflower_passenger)" title="John Carver (Mayflower passenger)">John Carver</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/William_Bradford_(Plymouth_Colony_governor)" title="William Bradford (Plymouth Colony governor)">William Bradford</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Miles_Standish" title="Miles Standish">Miles Standish</a>, at prayer during their voyage to North America. 1844 painting by <a href="/wiki/Robert_Walter_Weir" title="Robert Walter Weir">Robert Walter Weir</a></div></div></div>
<p>References to Moses were used by the <a href="/wiki/Puritans" title="Puritans">Puritans</a>, who relied on the story of Moses to give meaning and hope to the lives of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pilgrim_Fathers" title="Pilgrim Fathers">Pilgrims</a> seeking <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_religion" title="Freedom of religion">religious</a> and <a href="/wiki/Civil_liberties" title="Civil liberties">personal freedom</a> in North America. <a href="/wiki/John_Carver_(Plymouth_Colony_governor)" title="John Carver (Plymouth Colony governor)">John Carver</a> was the first governor of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plymouth_colony" title="Plymouth colony">Plymouth colony</a> and first signer of the <a href="/wiki/Mayflower_Compact" title="Mayflower Compact">Mayflower Compact</a>, which he wrote in 1620 during the ship <i><a href="/wiki/Mayflower" title="Mayflower">Mayflower</a>'</i>s three-month voyage. He inspired the Pilgrims with a "sense of earthly grandeur and divine purpose", notes historian <a href="/wiki/Jon_Meacham" title="Jon Meacham">Jon Meacham</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeacham200640_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeacham200640-172">[168]</a></sup> and was called the "Moses of the Pilgrims".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[169]</a></sup> Early American writer <a href="/wiki/James_Russell_Lowell" title="James Russell Lowell">James Russell Lowell</a> noted the similarity of the founding of America by the Pilgrims to that of <a href="/wiki/History_of_ancient_Israel_and_Judah" title="History of ancient Israel and Judah">ancient Israel</a> by Moses:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Next to the fugitives whom Moses led out of Egypt, the little shipload of outcasts who landed at Plymouth are destined to influence the future of the world. The spiritual thirst of mankind has for ages been quenched at Hebrew fountains; but the embodiment in human institutions of truths uttered by the <a href="/wiki/Son_of_man" title="Son of man">Son of Man</a> eighteen centuries ago was to be mainly the work of Puritan thought and Puritan self-devotion. ... If their municipal regulations smack somewhat of Judaism, yet there can be no nobler aim or more practical wisdom than theirs; for it was to make the law of man a living counterpart of the law of God, in their highest conception of it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lowell_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lowell-174">[170]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Following Carver's death the following year, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/William_Bradford_(Plymouth_Colony_governor)" title="William Bradford (Plymouth Colony governor)">William Bradford</a> was made governor. He feared that the remaining Pilgrims would not survive the hardships of the new land, with half their people having already died within months of arriving. Bradford evoked the symbol of Moses to the weakened and desperate Pilgrims to help calm them and give them hope: "Violence will break all. Where is the meek and humble spirit of Moses?"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[171]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/William_G._Dever" title="William G. Dever">William G. Dever</a> explains the attitude of the Pilgrims: "We considered ourselves the 'New Israel', particularly we in America. And for that reason we knew who we were, what we believed in and valued, and what our '<a href="/wiki/Manifest_destiny" title="Manifest destiny">manifest destiny</a>' was."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDever2006ix,_234_176-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDever2006ix,_234-176">[172]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[173]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Founding_Fathers_of_the_United_States">Founding Fathers of the United States</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:FirstCommitteeGreatSealReverseLossingDrawing.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1480" data-file-width="1480" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/FirstCommitteeGreatSealReverseLossingDrawing.jpg/220px-FirstCommitteeGreatSealReverseLossingDrawing.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/FirstCommitteeGreatSealReverseLossingDrawing.jpg/330px-FirstCommitteeGreatSealReverseLossingDrawing.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/FirstCommitteeGreatSealReverseLossingDrawing.jpg/440px-FirstCommitteeGreatSealReverseLossingDrawing.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:FirstCommitteeGreatSealReverseLossingDrawing.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>First proposed seal of the United States, 1776</div></div></div>
<p>On July 4, 1776, immediately after the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Declaration_of_Independence" title="United States Declaration of Independence">Declaration of Independence</a> was officially passed, the <a href="/wiki/Continental_Congress" title="Continental Congress">Continental Congress</a> asked <a href="/wiki/John_Adams" title="John Adams">John Adams</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Thomas Jefferson</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Franklin" title="Benjamin Franklin">Benjamin Franklin</a> to design a seal that would clearly represent a symbol for the new United States. They chose the symbol of Moses leading the Israelites to freedom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFeiler200935_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFeiler200935-178">[174]</a></sup>
</p><p>After the death of <a href="/wiki/George_Washington" title="George Washington">George Washington</a> in 1799, two thirds of his eulogies referred to him as "America's Moses", with one orator saying that "Washington has been the same to us as Moses was to the Children of Israel."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFeiler2009102_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFeiler2009102-179">[175]</a></sup>
</p><p>Benjamin Franklin, in 1788, saw the difficulties that some of the newly independent <a href="/wiki/U.S._state" title="U.S. state">American states</a> were having in forming a government, and proposed that until a new code of laws could be agreed to, they should be governed by "the laws of Moses", as contained in the Old Testament.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFranklin1834504_180-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFranklin1834504-180">[176]</a></sup> He justified his proposal by explaining that the laws had worked in biblical times: "The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Supreme_Being" title="Supreme Being">Supreme Being</a>Â ... having rescued them from bondage by many miracles, performed by his servant Moses, he personally delivered to that chosen servant, in the presence of the whole nation, a constitution and code of laws for their observance."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFranklin1834211_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFranklin1834211-181">[177]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/John_Adams" title="John Adams">John Adams</a>, 2nd <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_Presidents_of_the_United_States" title="List of Presidents of the United States">President of the United States</a>, stated why he relied on the laws of Moses over <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_philosophy" title="Ancient Greek philosophy">Greek philosophy</a> for establishing the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_Constitution" title="United States Constitution">United States Constitution</a>: "As much as I love, esteem, and admire the Greeks, I believe the Hebrews have done more to enlighten and civilize the world. Moses did more than all their legislators and philosophers."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeacham200640_172-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeacham200640-172">[168]</a></sup> Swedish historian <a href="/wiki/Hugo_Valentin" title="Hugo Valentin">Hugo Valentin</a> credited Moses as the "first to proclaim the <a href="/wiki/Human_rights" title="Human rights">rights of man</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shuldiner_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shuldiner-182">[178]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Slavery_and_civil_rights">Slavery and civil rights</span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Underground_Railroad" title="Underground Railroad">Underground Railroad</a> conductor and <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a> veteran <a href="/wiki/Harriet_Tubman" title="Harriet Tubman">Harriet Tubman</a> was nicknamed "Moses" due to her various missions in freeing and ferrying escaped enslaved persons to freedom in the free states of the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[179]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[180]</a></sup>
</p><p>Historian Gladys L. Knight describes how leaders who emerged during and after the period in which <a href="/wiki/Slavery_in_the_United_States" title="Slavery in the United States">slavery</a> was legal often personified the Moses symbol. "The symbol of Moses was empowering in that it served to amplify a need for freedom."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[181]</a></sup> Therefore, when <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Lincoln" title="Abraham Lincoln">Abraham Lincoln</a> was <a href="/wiki/Assassination_of_Abraham_Lincoln" title="Assassination of Abraham Lincoln">assassinated in 1865</a> after the passage of the <a href="/wiki/Thirteenth_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Thirteenth Amendment to the United States Constitution">amendment to the Constitution outlawing slavery</a>, <a href="/wiki/African_Americans" title="African Americans">Black Americans</a> said they had lost "their Moses".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[182]</a></sup> Lincoln biographer <a href="/wiki/Charles_Carleton_Coffin" title="Charles Carleton Coffin">Charles Carleton Coffin</a> writes, "The millions whom Abraham Lincoln delivered from slavery will ever liken him to Moses, the deliverer of Israel."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[183]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 1960s, a leading figure in the <a href="/wiki/Civil_rights_movement" title="Civil rights movement">civil rights movement</a> was <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther_King_Jr." title="Martin Luther King Jr.">Martin Luther King Jr.</a>, who was called "a modern Moses", and often referred to Moses in his speeches: "The struggle of Moses, the struggle of his devoted followers as they sought to get out of Egypt. This is something of the story of every people struggling for freedom."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[184]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural_portrayals_and_references">Cultural portrayals and references</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Art">Art</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%27Moses%27_by_Michelangelo_JBU160.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4127" data-file-width="2751" decoding="async" height="330" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/%27Moses%27_by_Michelangelo_JBU160.jpg/220px-%27Moses%27_by_Michelangelo_JBU160.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/%27Moses%27_by_Michelangelo_JBU160.jpg/330px-%27Moses%27_by_Michelangelo_JBU160.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/%27Moses%27_by_Michelangelo_JBU160.jpg/440px-%27Moses%27_by_Michelangelo_JBU160.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%27Moses%27_by_Michelangelo_JBU160.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Moses_(Michelangelo)" title="Moses (Michelangelo)">Moses</a></i>, with horns, by <a href="/wiki/Michelangelo" title="Michelangelo">Michelangelo</a>, 1513â1515, in Basilica San Pietro in Vincoli, Rome</div></div></div>
<p>Moses often appears in Christian art, and the Pope's private chapel, the <a href="/wiki/Sistine_Chapel" title="Sistine Chapel">Sistine Chapel</a>, has a <a href="/wiki/Sistine_Chapel#Southern_wall" title="Sistine Chapel">large sequence of six frescos</a> of the <i>life of Moses</i> on the southern wall, opposite a set with the <i>life of Christ</i>. They were painted in 1481â82 by a group of mostly Florentine artists including <a href="/wiki/Sandro_Botticelli" title="Sandro Botticelli">Sandro Botticelli</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pietro_Perugino" title="Pietro Perugino">Pietro Perugino</a>. Because of an ambiguity in <a href="/wiki/Jerome" title="Jerome">Jerome</a>'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_Vulgate" title="Latin Vulgate">Latin Vulgate</a> translation of the Bible, where Moses's face is described as <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">cornutam</i></span> (meaning either "shining" or "horned") when descending from Mount Sinai with the tablets, Moses is usually shown in Western art until the Renaissance with small horns, which at least served as a convenient identifying attribute.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[185]</a></sup>
</p><p>With the prophet <a href="/wiki/Elijah" title="Elijah">Elijah</a>, he is a necessary figure in the <a href="/wiki/Transfiguration_of_Jesus_in_Christian_art" title="Transfiguration of Jesus in Christian art">Transfiguration of Jesus in Christian art</a>, a subject with a long history in Eastern Orthodox art, and popular in Western art between about 1475 and 1535.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[186]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Michelangelo.27s_statue"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Michelangelo's_statue">Michelangelo's statue</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Moses_(Michelangelo)" title="Moses (Michelangelo)">Moses (Michelangelo)</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Michelangelo" title="Michelangelo">Michelangelo</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Moses_(Michelangelo)" title="Moses (Michelangelo)">statue of Moses</a> (1513â1515), in the Church of <a href="/wiki/San_Pietro_in_Vincoli" title="San Pietro in Vincoli">San Pietro in Vincoli</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rome" title="Rome">Rome</a>, is one of the most familiar statues in the world. The horns the sculptor included on Moses's head are the result of a mistranslation of the Hebrew Bible into the Latin <a href="/wiki/Vulgate" title="Vulgate">Vulgate Bible</a> with which Michelangelo was familiar. The Hebrew word taken from <i>Exodus</i> means either a "horn" or an "irradiation". Experts at the <a href="/wiki/Archaeological_Institute_of_America" title="Archaeological Institute of America">Archaeological Institute of America</a> show that the term was used when Moses "returned to his people after seeing as much of the Glory of the Lord as human eye could stand", and his face "reflected radiance".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[187]</a></sup> In early <a href="/wiki/Jewish_culture#Visual_arts_and_architecture" title="Jewish culture">Jewish art</a>, moreover, Moses is often "shown with rays coming out of his head".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[188]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Depiction_on_U.S._government_buildings">Depiction on U.S. government buildings</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Moses_bas-relief_in_the_U.S._House_of_Representatives_chamber.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="318" decoding="async" height="277" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Moses_bas-relief_in_the_U.S._House_of_Representatives_chamber.jpg/220px-Moses_bas-relief_in_the_U.S._House_of_Representatives_chamber.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/38/Moses_bas-relief_in_the_U.S._House_of_Representatives_chamber.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Moses_bas-relief_in_the_U.S._House_of_Representatives_chamber.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sculpture in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._House_of_Representatives" title="U.S. House of Representatives">U.S. House of Representatives</a></div></div></div>
<p>Moses is depicted in several U.S. government buildings because of his legacy as a lawgiver. In the <a href="/wiki/Library_of_Congress" title="Library of Congress">Library of Congress</a> stands a large statue of Moses alongside a statue of the <a href="/wiki/Paul_the_Apostle" title="Paul the Apostle">Paul the Apostle</a>. Moses is one of the 23 lawgivers depicted in <a href="/wiki/Marble" title="Marble">marble</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bas-relief" title="Bas-relief">bas-reliefs</a> in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Capitol#House_Chamber" title="United States Capitol">chamber</a> of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="United States House of Representatives">U.S. House of Representatives</a> in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Capitol" title="United States Capitol">United States Capitol</a>. The plaque's overview states: "Moses (c.Â 1350â1250Â B.C.) Hebrew prophet and lawgiver; transformed a wandering people into a nation; received the Ten Commandments."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[189]</a></sup>
</p><p>The other 22 figures have their profiles turned to Moses, which is the only forward-facing bas-relief.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[190]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[191]</a></sup>
</p><p>Moses appears eight times in carvings that ring the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Supreme_Court_Building" title="United States Supreme Court Building">Supreme Court Great Hall</a> ceiling. His face is presented along with other ancient figures such as <a href="/wiki/Solomon" title="Solomon">Solomon</a>, the Greek god <a href="/wiki/Zeus" title="Zeus">Zeus</a>, and the Roman goddess of wisdom, <a href="/wiki/Minerva" title="Minerva">Minerva</a>. The Supreme Court Building's east pediment depicts Moses holding two tablets. Tablets representing the Ten Commandments can be found carved in the oak courtroom doors, on the support frame of the courtroom's bronze gates, and in the library woodwork. A controversial image is one that sits directly above the <a href="/wiki/Chief_Justice_of_the_United_States" title="Chief Justice of the United States">Chief Justice of the United States</a>' head. In the center of the 40-foot-long Spanish marble carving is a tablet displaying <a href="/wiki/Roman_numerals" title="Roman numerals">Roman numerals</a> I through X, with some numbers partially hidden.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[192]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Literature">Literature</span></h3>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Sigmund_Freud" title="Sigmund Freud">Sigmund Freud</a>, in his last book, <i><a href="/wiki/Moses_and_Monotheism" title="Moses and Monotheism">Moses and Monotheism</a></i> in 1939, postulated that Moses was an Egyptian nobleman who adhered to the <a href="/wiki/Monotheism" title="Monotheism">monotheism</a> of <a href="/wiki/Akhenaten" title="Akhenaten">Akhenaten</a>. Following a theory proposed by a contemporary <a href="/wiki/Biblical_criticism" title="Biblical criticism">biblical critic</a>, Freud believed that Moses was murdered in the wilderness, producing a collective sense of <a href="/wiki/Patricide" title="Patricide">patricidal</a> guilt that has been at the heart of Judaism ever since. "Judaism had been a religion of the father, Christianity became a religion of the son", he wrote. The possible Egyptian origin of Moses and of his message has received significant scholarly attention.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAssmann1997_197-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAssmann1997-197">[193]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (September 2015)">pageÂ needed</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[194]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources#What_information_to_include" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="A complete citation is needed. (May 2022)">full citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Opponents of this view observe that the religion of the Torah seems different from <a href="/wiki/Atenism" title="Atenism">Atenism</a> in everything except the central feature of devotion to a single god,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[195]</a></sup> although this has been countered by a variety of arguments, e.g. pointing out the similarities between the <a href="/wiki/Great_Hymn_to_the_Aten" title="Great Hymn to the Aten">Hymn to Aten</a> and <a href="/wiki/Psalm_104" title="Psalm 104">Psalm 104</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAssmann1997_197-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAssmann1997-197">[193]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (September 2015)">pageÂ needed</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[196]</a></sup> Freud's interpretation of the historical Moses is not well accepted among <a href="/wiki/Historian" title="Historian">historians</a>, and is considered <a href="/wiki/Pseudohistory" title="Pseudohistory">pseudohistory</a> by many.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[197]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (September 2015)">pageÂ needed</span></a></i>]</sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Thomas_Mann" title="Thomas Mann">Thomas Mann</a>'s novella <i><a href="/wiki/The_Tables_of_the_Law" title="The Tables of the Law">The Tables of the Law</a></i> (1944) is a retelling of the story of the Exodus from Egypt, with Moses as its main character.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202"><a href="#cite_note-202">[198]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/W._G._Hardy" title="W. G. Hardy">W. G. Hardy</a>'s novel <i>All the Trumpets Sounded</i> (1942) tells a fictionalized life of Moses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[199]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Orson_Scott_Card" title="Orson Scott Card">Orson Scott Card</a>'s novel <i><a href="/wiki/Stone_Tables" title="Stone Tables">Stone Tables</a></i> (1997) is a novelization of the life of Moses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204"><a href="#cite_note-204">[200]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Film_and_television">Film and television</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Charlton_Heston_in_The_Ten_Commandments_film_trailer.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1366" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Charlton_Heston_in_The_Ten_Commandments_film_trailer.jpg/220px-Charlton_Heston_in_The_Ten_Commandments_film_trailer.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Charlton_Heston_in_The_Ten_Commandments_film_trailer.jpg/330px-Charlton_Heston_in_The_Ten_Commandments_film_trailer.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Charlton_Heston_in_The_Ten_Commandments_film_trailer.jpg/440px-Charlton_Heston_in_The_Ten_Commandments_film_trailer.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Charlton_Heston_in_The_Ten_Commandments_film_trailer.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Charlton_Heston" title="Charlton Heston">Charlton Heston</a> in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Ten_Commandments_(1956_film)" title="The Ten Commandments (1956 film)">The Ten Commandments</a></i>, 1956</div></div></div>
<ul><li>Moses was portrayed by <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Roberts" title="Theodore Roberts">Theodore Roberts</a> in <a href="/wiki/Cecil_B._DeMille" title="Cecil B. DeMille">Cecil B. DeMille</a>'s 1923 <a href="/wiki/Silent_film" title="Silent film">silent film</a> <i><a href="/wiki/The_Ten_Commandments_(1923_film)" title="The Ten Commandments (1923 film)">The Ten Commandments</a></i>. Moses also appeared as the central character in the 1956 remake, also directed by DeMille and called <i><a href="/wiki/The_Ten_Commandments_(1956_film)" title="The Ten Commandments (1956 film)">The Ten Commandments</a></i>, in which he was portrayed by <a href="/wiki/Charlton_Heston" title="Charlton Heston">Charlton Heston</a>, who had a noted resemblance to Michelangelo's statue. A <a href="/wiki/The_Ten_Commandments_(miniseries)" title="The Ten Commandments (miniseries)">television remake</a> was produced in 2006.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205"><a href="#cite_note-205">[201]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[202]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Burt_Lancaster" title="Burt Lancaster">Burt Lancaster</a> played <i>Moses</i> in the 1975 television <a href="/wiki/Miniseries" title="Miniseries">miniseries</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Moses_the_Lawgiver" title="Moses the Lawgiver">Moses the Lawgiver</a></i>.</li>
<li>In the 1981 <a href="/wiki/Comedy_film" title="Comedy film">comedy film</a> <i><a href="/wiki/History_of_the_World,_Part_I" title="History of the World, Part I">History of the World, Part I</a></i>, Moses was portrayed by <a href="/wiki/Mel_Brooks" title="Mel Brooks">Mel Brooks</a>.</li>
<li>In 1995, Sir <a href="/wiki/Ben_Kingsley" title="Ben Kingsley">Ben Kingsley</a> portrayed Moses in the 1995 TV film <a href="/wiki/Moses_(miniseries)" title="Moses (miniseries)"><i>Moses</i></a>, produced by British and Italian production companies.</li>
<li>Moses appeared as the central character in the 1998 <a href="/wiki/DreamWorks_Pictures" title="DreamWorks Pictures">DreamWorks Pictures</a> animated film <i><a href="/wiki/The_Prince_of_Egypt" title="The Prince of Egypt">The Prince of Egypt</a></i>. His speaking voice was provided by <a href="/wiki/Val_Kilmer" title="Val Kilmer">Val Kilmer</a>, with American gospel singer and tenor <a href="/wiki/Amick_Byram" title="Amick Byram">Amick Byram</a> providing his singing voice.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ben_Kingsley" title="Ben Kingsley">Ben Kingsley</a> was the narrator of the 2007 animated film <i><a href="/wiki/The_Ten_Commandments_(2007_film)" title="The Ten Commandments (2007 film)">The Ten Commandments</a></i>.</li>
<li>In the 2009 <a href="/wiki/Miniseries" title="Miniseries">miniseries</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Battles_BC" title="Battles BC">Battles BC</a></i>, Moses was portrayed by <a href="/wiki/Cazzey_Louis_Cereghino" title="Cazzey Louis Cereghino">Cazzey Louis Cereghino</a>.</li>
<li>In the 2013 television miniseries <i><a href="/wiki/The_Bible_(miniseries)" title="The Bible (miniseries)">The Bible</a></i>, Moses was portrayed by <a href="/wiki/William_Houston_(actor)" title="William Houston (actor)">William Houston</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Christian_Bale" title="Christian Bale">Christian Bale</a> portrayed Moses in <a href="/wiki/Ridley_Scott" title="Ridley Scott">Ridley Scott</a>'s 2014 film <i><a href="/wiki/Exodus:_Gods_and_Kings" title="Exodus: Gods and Kings">Exodus: Gods and Kings</a></i> which portrayed Moses and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rameses_II" title="Rameses II">Rameses II</a> as being raised by <a href="/wiki/Seti_I" title="Seti I">Seti I</a> as cousins.</li>
<li>The 2016 Brazilian Biblical telenovela <i><a href="/wiki/Os_Dez_Mandamentos" title="Os Dez Mandamentos">Os Dez Mandamentos</a></i> features Brazilian actor <a href="/wiki/Guilherme_Winter" title="Guilherme Winter">Guilherme Winter</a> portraying Moses.</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Criticism_of_Moses">Criticism of Moses</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tissot_The_Women_of_Midian_Led_Captive_by_the_Hebrews.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="359" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="132" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Tissot_The_Women_of_Midian_Led_Captive_by_the_Hebrews.jpg/220px-Tissot_The_Women_of_Midian_Led_Captive_by_the_Hebrews.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Tissot_The_Women_of_Midian_Led_Captive_by_the_Hebrews.jpg/330px-Tissot_The_Women_of_Midian_Led_Captive_by_the_Hebrews.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Tissot_The_Women_of_Midian_Led_Captive_by_the_Hebrews.jpg/440px-Tissot_The_Women_of_Midian_Led_Captive_by_the_Hebrews.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tissot_The_Women_of_Midian_Led_Captive_by_the_Hebrews.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>The Women of Midian Led Captive by the Hebrews,</i> <a href="/wiki/James_Tissot" title="James Tissot">James Tissot</a> c.1900</div></div></div>
<p>In the late eighteenth century, the deist <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Paine" title="Thomas Paine">Thomas Paine</a> commented at length on Moses's Laws in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Age_of_Reason" title="The Age of Reason">The Age of Reason</a></i> (1794, 1795, and 1807). Paine considered Moses to be a "detestable <a href="/wiki/Villain" title="Villain">villain</a>", and cited Numbers 31 as an example of his "unexampled atrocities".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207"><a href="#cite_note-207">[203]</a></sup> In the passage, after the Israelite army returned from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Midian_war" title="Midian war">conquering Midian</a>, Moses orders the killing of the Midianites with the exception of the virgin girls who were to be kept for the Israelites.
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Have ye saved all the women alive? behold, these caused the children of Israel, through the counsel of <a href="/wiki/Balaam" title="Balaam">Balaam</a>, to commit trespass against the Lord in the matter of <a href="/wiki/Peor" title="Peor">Peor</a>, and there was a plague among the congregation of the Lord. Now, therefore, kill every male among the little ones, and kill every woman that hath known a man by lying with him; but all the women-children, that <a href="/wiki/Virginity" title="Virginity">have not known a man by lying with him</a>, keep alive for yourselves.</p><div class="templatequotecite">ââ<cite>Numbers 31<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208"><a href="#cite_note-208">[204]</a></sup></cite></div></blockquote>
<p>Rabbi Joel Grossman argued that the story is a "powerful <a href="/wiki/Fable" title="Fable">fable</a> of <a href="/wiki/Lust" title="Lust">lust</a> and <a href="/wiki/Betrayal" title="Betrayal">betrayal</a>", and that Moses's execution of the women was a symbolic condemnation of those who seek to turn sex and desire to evil purposes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[205]</a></sup> He says that the Middianite women "used their sexual attractiveness to turn the Israelite men away from [Yahweh] God and toward the worship of Baal Peor [another Canaanite God]".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_210-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-210">[206]</a></sup> Rabbi Grossman argues that the genocide of all the Middianite non-virgin women, including those that did not seduce Jewish men, was fair because some of them had sex for "improper reasons".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_210-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-210">[206]</a></sup> Alan Levin, an educational specialist with the <a href="/wiki/Reform_Judaism" title="Reform Judaism">Reform</a> movement, has similarly suggested that the story should be taken as a <a href="/wiki/Cautionary_tale" title="Cautionary tale">cautionary tale</a>, to "warn successive generations of Jews to watch their own idolatrous behavior".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-211"><a href="#cite_note-211">[207]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Moses_Sofer" title="Moses Sofer">Chasam Sofer</a> emphasizes that this war was not fought at Moses's behest, but was commanded by God as an act of revenge against the Midianite women,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-212"><a href="#cite_note-212">[208]</a></sup> who, according to the Biblical account, had seduced the Israelites and led them to sin.
</p><p>Moses has also been the subject of much feminist criticism. <a href="/wiki/Womanist_theology" title="Womanist theology">Womanist Biblical</a> scholar <a href="/wiki/Nyasha_Junior" title="Nyasha Junior">Nyasha Junior</a> has argued that Moses can be the object of feminist inquiry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-213"><a href="#cite_note-213">[209]</a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-15"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-15">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFNigosian1993">Nigosian, S.A. (1993). "Moses as They Saw Him". <i>Vetus Testamentum</i>. <b>43</b> (3): 339â350. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1163%2F156853393X00160" rel="nofollow">10.1163/156853393X00160</a>. <q>Three views, based on source analysis or historical-critical method, seem to prevail among biblical scholars. First, a number of scholars, such as Meyer and Holscher, aim to deprive Moses all the prerogatives attributed to him by denying anything historical value about his person or the role he played in Israelite religion. Second, other scholars,.... diametrically oppose the first view and strive to anchor Moses the decisive role he played in Israelite religion in a firm setting. And third, those who take the middle position... delineate the solidly historical identification of Moses from the superstructure of later legendary accretionsâ¦.Needless to say, these issues are hotly debated unresolved matters among scholars. Thus, the attempt to separate the historical from unhistorical elements in the Torah has yielded few, if any, positive results regarding the figure of Moses or the role he played on Israelite religion. No wonder J. Van Seters concluded that "the quest for the historical Moses is a futile exercise. He now belongs only to legend</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Vetus+Testamentum&amp;rft.atitle=Moses+as+They+Saw+Him&amp;rft.volume=43&amp;rft.issue=3&amp;rft.pages=339-350&amp;rft.date=1993&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1163%2F156853393X00160&amp;rft.aulast=Nigosian&amp;rft.aufirst=S.A.&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AMoses"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-Hays-26"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Hays_26-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Hays_26-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Hays_26-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Hays, Christopher B. 2014. <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.it/books?id=r5W7BwAAQBAJ&amp;pg=PA116" rel="nofollow"><i>Hidden Riches: A Sourcebook for the Comparative Study of the Hebrew Bible and Ancient Near East</i></a>. <a href="/wiki/Presbyterian_Publishing_Corporation" title="Presbyterian Publishing Corporation">Presbyterian Publishing</a>. p. 116.</span>

<li id="cite_note-27"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-27">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Kenneth A. Kitchen, <i>On the Reliability of the Old Testament</i> (2003), pp. 296â97: "His name is widely held to be Egyptian, and its form is too often misinterpreted by biblical scholars. It is frequently equated with the Egyptian word 'ms' (Mose) meaning 'child', and stated to be an abbreviation of a name compounded with that of a deity whose name has been omitted. And indeed we have many Egyptians called Amen-mose, Ptah-mose, Ra-mose, Hor-mose, and so on. But this explanation is wrong. We also have very many Egyptians who were actually called just 'Mose', without omission of any particular deity. Most famous because of his family's long lawsuit in the middle-class scribe Mose (of the temple of Ptah at Memphis), under Ramesses II; but he had many homonyms. So, the omission-of-deity explanation is to be dismissed as wrongÂ ... There is worse. The name of Moses is most likely not Egyptian in the first place! The sibilants do not match as they should, and this cannot be explained away. Overwhelmingly, Egyptian 's' appears as 's' (samekh) in Hebrew and West Semitic, while Hebrew and West Semitic 's' (samekh) appears as 'tj' in Egyptian. Conversely, Egyptian 'sh' = Hebrew 'sh', and vice versa. It is better to admit that the child was named (Exod 2:10b) by his own mother, in a form originally vocalized 'Mashu', 'one drawn out' (which became 'Moshe', 'he who draws out', i.e., his people from slavery, when he led them forth). In fourteenth/thirteenth-century Egypt, 'Mose' was actually pronounced 'Masu', and so it is perfectly possible that a young Hebrew Mashu was nicknamed Masu by his Egyptian companions; but this is a verbal pun, not a borrowing either way."</span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-59"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-59">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFRobinson2008">Robinson, George (2008). <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=X54NS-Lc-OcC&amp;q=%22dictated+to+Moshe+by+God%22%22under+the+inspiration+of+God%22&amp;pg=PA97" rel="nofollow"><i>Essential Torah: A Complete Guide to the Five Books of Moses</i></a>. Knopf Doubleday Publishing Group. p.Â 97. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a>Â <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-307-48437-6" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-307-48437-6"><bdi>978-0-307-48437-6</bdi></a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Essential+Torah%3A+A+Complete+Guide+to+the+Five+Books+of+Moses&amp;rft.pages=97&amp;rft.pub=Knopf+Doubleday+Publishing+Group&amp;rft.date=2008&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-307-48437-6&amp;rft.aulast=Robinson&amp;rft.aufirst=George&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DX54NS-Lc-OcC%26q%3D%2522dictated%2Bto%2BMoshe%2Bby%2BGod%2522%2522under%2Bthe%2Binspiration%2Bof%2BGod%2522%26pg%3DPA97&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AMoses"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-Nigo1993-60"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Nigo1993_60-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Nigo1993_60-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFNigosian1993">Nigosian, S. A. (1993). "Moses as They Saw Him". <i>Vetus Testamentum</i>. <b>43</b> (3): 339â350. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1163%2F156853393X00160" rel="nofollow">10.1163/156853393X00160</a>. <q>Three views, based on source analysis or historical-critical method, seem to prevail among biblical scholars. First, a number of scholars, such as Meyer and Holscher, aim to deprive Moses all the prerogatives attributed to him by denying anything historical value about his person or the role he played in Israelite religion. Second, other scholars,Â ... diametrically oppose the first view and strive to anchor Moses the decisive role he played in Israelite religion in a firm setting. And third, those who take the middle positionÂ ... delineate the solidly historical identification of Moses from the superstructure of later legendary accretionsÂ ... Needless to say, these issues are hotly debated unresolved matters among scholars. Thus, the attempt to separate the historical from unhistorical elements in the Torah has yielded few, if any, positive results regarding the figure of Moses or the role he played on Israelite religion. No wonder J. Van Seters concluded that 'the quest for the historical Moses is a futile exercise. He now belongs only to legend.'</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Vetus+Testamentum&amp;rft.atitle=Moses+as+They+Saw+Him&amp;rft.volume=43&amp;rft.issue=3&amp;rft.pages=339-350&amp;rft.date=1993&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1163%2F156853393X00160&amp;rft.aulast=Nigosian&amp;rft.aufirst=S.+A.&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AMoses"></span></span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-Dever_2008-65"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Dever_2008_65-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation web cs1" id="CITEREFDever2008">Dever, William (November 17, 2008). <a class="external text" href="https://www.pbs.org/wgbh/nova/article/archeology-hebrew-bible/" rel="nofollow">"Archeology of the Hebrew Bible"</a>. <i>Nova</i>. <li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEKeeler200555â66-149"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKeeler200555â66_149-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKeeler200555â66_149-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKeeler200555â66_149-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKeeler200555â66_149-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFKeeler2005">Keeler 2005</a>, pp.Â 55â66.</span>

<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEKeeler200555â56-150"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKeeler200555â56_150-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFKeeler2005">Keeler 2005</a>, pp.Â 55â56, describes Moses from the Muslim perspective: <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Among prophets, Moses has been described as the one "whose career as a messenger of God, lawgiver and leader of his community most closely parallels and foreshadows that of Muhammad", and as "the figure that in the Koran was presented to Muhammad above all others as the supreme model of saviour and ruler of a community, the man chosen to present both knowledge of the one God, and a divinely revealed system of law". We find him clearly in this role of Muhammad's forebear in a well-known tradition of the miraculous ascension of the Prophet, where Moses advises Muhammad from his own experience as messenger and lawgiver.</p></blockquote></span>
